\section{Global $U(1)$ Bundle from the Projective Order Parameter}

Let the low-energy orientational degree of freedom be represented by
\begin{equation}
z(x)\in \mathbb{C}^3,
\qquad
z^\dagger z = 1,
\qquad
z(x)\sim e^{i\alpha(x)} z(x).
\end{equation}
Because physically equivalent configurations differ by a local phase,
the true target space is not $S^5$ but the projective space
\begin{equation}
\mathbb{C}P^2 \cong S^5/U(1).
\end{equation}
Hence the normalized field $z$ is a local section of the principal $U(1)$ bundle
\begin{equation}
U(1)\hookrightarrow S^5 \xrightarrow{\ \pi\ } \mathbb{C}P^2.
\end{equation}

Equivalently, the emergent orientational sector defines the Hopf-type principal bundle
over $\mathbb{C}P^2$. Therefore the $U(1)$ phase is not an additional physical mode,
but the fiber coordinate of the bundle, while the physical configuration is the base point
$[z]\in \mathbb{C}P^2$.

\section{Local Trivializations and Transition Functions}

Let $\{U_i\}$ be an open cover of $\mathbb{C}P^2$ such that on each patch
one can choose a local representative $z_{(i)}$ of the equivalence class $[z]$.
On overlaps $U_i\cap U_j\neq\varnothing$, the representatives differ by a phase:
\begin{equation}
z_{(j)} = e^{i\alpha_{ij}} z_{(i)}.
\end{equation}
The functions
\begin{equation}
g_{ij} := e^{i\alpha_{ij}} : U_i\cap U_j \to U(1)
\end{equation}
satisfy the cocycle conditions
\begin{equation}
g_{ii}=1,
\qquad
g_{ij}g_{jk}g_{ki}=1
\quad
\text{on } U_i\cap U_j\cap U_k.
\end{equation}
Therefore $\{g_{ij}\}$ defines a bona fide global principal $U(1)$ bundle.

\section{Natural Berry Connection and Emergent Abelian Gauge Field}

On each local section $z_{(i)}$, define the one-form
\begin{equation}
A^{(i)}_\mu := -\, i\, z_{(i)}^\dagger \partial_\mu z_{(i)}.
\end{equation}
Using $z_{(j)} = e^{i\alpha_{ij}} z_{(i)}$, one obtains
\begin{equation}
A^{(j)}_\mu
=
-\, i\, z_{(j)}^\dagger \partial_\mu z_{(j)}
=
A^{(i)}_\mu + \partial_\mu \alpha_{ij}.
\end{equation}
Thus $A_\mu$ is a $U(1)$ connection on the principal bundle.

The gauge-covariant derivative is
\begin{equation}
D_\mu z := (\partial_\mu - i A_\mu) z,
\end{equation}
and transforms covariantly:
\begin{equation}
z \to e^{i\alpha} z,
\qquad
A_\mu \to A_\mu + \partial_\mu \alpha,
\qquad
D_\mu z \to e^{i\alpha} D_\mu z.
\end{equation}
Hence the projective kinetic term is globally well-defined:
\begin{equation}
(D_\mu z)^\dagger (D^\mu z)
=
\partial_\mu z^\dagger \partial^\mu z - |z^\dagger\partial_\mu z|^2.
\end{equation}

\section{Global Curvature Two-Form}

Define the curvature on each patch by
\begin{equation}
F^{(i)}_{\mu\nu}
:=
\partial_\mu A^{(i)}_\nu - \partial_\nu A^{(i)}_\mu.
\end{equation}
Since the transition is Abelian,
\begin{equation}
F^{(j)}_{\mu\nu} = F^{(i)}_{\mu\nu}
\quad
\text{on } U_i\cap U_j,
\end{equation}
so the curvature patches together into a globally defined two-form
\begin{equation}
F = dA.
\end{equation}
Explicitly,
\begin{equation}
F_{\mu\nu}
=
-\, i\,
\Big(
\partial_\mu z^\dagger \partial_\nu z
-
\partial_\nu z^\dagger \partial_\mu z
\Big).
\end{equation}
Therefore $F_{\mu\nu}$ is gauge-invariant and globally defined, even though $A_\mu$
itself is only local.

\section{First Chern Class and Flux Quantization}

Because $F$ is the curvature of a principal $U(1)$ bundle over the physical base manifold,
its de Rham cohomology class is quantized:
\begin{equation}
\frac{1}{2\pi}\int_{\Sigma_2} F \in \mathbb{Z}
\end{equation}
for every closed oriented two-cycle $\Sigma_2$ on which the bundle is defined.
This integer is the first Chern number
\begin{equation}
c_1 = \left[\frac{F}{2\pi}\right]\in H^2(\mathcal{M}_{\rm phys},\mathbb{Z}),
\end{equation}
and provides the topological charge carried by the Abelian bundle sector.

\section{Topological Defects: Homotopy Classification}

The physical target space is $\mathbb{C}P^2$, not $S^1$.
Therefore defect classification must be performed using homotopy groups of $\mathbb{C}P^2$.

For line defects in three spatial dimensions, the relevant group is
\begin{equation}
\pi_1(\mathbb{C}P^2)=0.
\end{equation}
Hence there is no topologically protected bulk vortex line associated with the orientational
order parameter itself.

For point defects in three spatial dimensions, one surrounds the defect by a sphere
$S^2_{\rm space}$ and classifies maps
\begin{equation}
S^2_{\rm space}\to \mathbb{C}P^2.
\end{equation}
The relevant group is
\begin{equation}
\pi_2(\mathbb{C}P^2)=\mathbb{Z}.
\end{equation}
Thus static point defects are topologically quantized by an integer charge $Q$.

A convenient representative formula is
\begin{equation}
Q
=
\frac{1}{2\pi}\int_{S^2_{\rm space}} F,
\end{equation}
which identifies the defect charge with the first Chern number of the pulled-back $U(1)$ bundle.
Therefore the natural stable defects of the emergent Abelian sector are monopole/hedgehog-type
point defects, not fundamental vortex lines.

\section{Embedded $\mathbb{C}P^1$ Sector and Minimal Defects}

Any fixed two-component subspace of $\mathbb{C}^3$ defines an embedded
\begin{equation}
\mathbb{C}P^1 \hookrightarrow \mathbb{C}P^2.
\end{equation}
On this submanifold the order parameter reduces to the usual two-component projective field,
and the charge becomes the standard sigma-model lump number.
Hence the minimal nontrivial defect sector is already captured by an embedded $\mathbb{C}P^1$,
while $\mathbb{C}P^2$ provides the full orientational completion.

\section{Gauge Consistency at the Geometric Level}

The emergent Abelian gauge structure is geometrically consistent if and only if:

\begin{enumerate}
\item Local representatives are related by $U(1)$ transition functions satisfying the cocycle condition.
\item The connection transforms as
\begin{equation}
A^{(j)}_\mu = A^{(i)}_\mu + \partial_\mu \alpha_{ij}.
\end{equation}
\item The curvature is globally defined:
\begin{equation}
F^{(j)}_{\mu\nu}=F^{(i)}_{\mu\nu}.
\end{equation}
\item The Bianchi identity holds identically:
\begin{equation}
\partial_{[\lambda}F_{\mu\nu]}=0.
\end{equation}
\item Topological charge is quantized:
\begin{equation}
\frac{1}{2\pi}\int_{\Sigma_2}F \in \mathbb{Z}.
\end{equation}
\end{enumerate}

All five conditions are automatic consequences of the projective definition
$z\sim e^{i\alpha}z$.
Therefore the $U(1)$ gauge sector is not postulated but forced by the global bundle structure.

\section{Gauge Consistency at the Dynamical Level}

The purely geometric bundle construction yields a consistent Abelian connection,
but a genuine low-energy gauge field additionally requires dynamical consistency.

At the effective level, the admissible infrared action is
\begin{equation}
S_{\rm eff}
=
\int d^4x
\left[
f^2 (D_\mu z)^\dagger (D^\mu z)
+
\frac{1}{4e^2}F_{\mu\nu}F^{\mu\nu}
+
\cdots
\right].
\end{equation}
A Proca mass term
\begin{equation}
\frac{m_A^2}{2} A_\mu A^\mu
\end{equation}
is not gauge-invariant under
$A_\mu\to A_\mu+\partial_\mu\alpha$ and therefore cannot survive on the exact gauge-consistent
infrared surface.

Thus the Abelian sector is dynamically consistent precisely when the RG flow drives
\begin{equation}
m_{A,k}^2 \longrightarrow 0
\qquad
(k\to 0),
\end{equation}
so that only the transverse modes remain propagating.
This is the Abelian analogue of the mWI/mSTI criterion used for the non-Abelian emergent gauge sector.

\section{Closed Theorem for the $U(1)$ Chain}

Starting from the normalized orientational field
$z\in \mathbb{C}^3$ with projective identification $z\sim e^{i\alpha}z$,
the following chain is mathematically closed:
\begin{equation}
z \in S^5
\quad \Longrightarrow \quad
S^5 \to \mathbb{C}P^2
\quad \Longrightarrow \quad
U(1)\text{ principal bundle}
\quad \Longrightarrow \quad
A_\mu = -i z^\dagger \partial_\mu z
\quad \Longrightarrow \quad
F_{\mu\nu} = \partial_\mu A_\nu - \partial_\nu A_\mu.
\end{equation}
Moreover,
\begin{equation}
\pi_1(\mathbb{C}P^2)=0,
\qquad
\pi_2(\mathbb{C}P^2)=\mathbb{Z},
\end{equation}
so the stable defects are integer-charged point defects classified by
\begin{equation}
Q=\frac{1}{2\pi}\int_{S^2}F\in\mathbb{Z}.
\end{equation}
Finally, infrared gauge consistency requires
\begin{equation}
m_{A,0}^2=0,
\end{equation}
leaving a genuine massless transverse $U(1)$ gauge mode.

%

\section{Explicit Hedgehog/Lump Ansatz for Point Defects in $\mathbb{C}P^2$ and its Interaction with the TEC Elastic Background}

\subsection{Point Defect Ansatz in $\mathbb{C}P^2$}

The point defect in $\mathbb{C}P^2$ is represented by the following ansatz:
\begin{equation}
z(r) = \left( \begin{array}{c}
\sin f(r) e^{i\theta} \\
\cos f(r)
\end{array} \right),
\end{equation}
where \(f(r)\) is a radial function that characterizes the defect profile.

\subsection{Elastic Energy of the TEC Background}

The elastic energy density in the TEC background is given by:
\begin{equation}
f_{\text{el}} = \frac{1}{2} C_{11} \epsilon_{ii}^2 + C_{12} \epsilon_{ii} \epsilon_{jj} + 2 C_{44} \epsilon_{ij}^2,
\end{equation}
where \(\epsilon_{ij}\) is the strain tensor, which will include contributions from the defect.

\subsection{Energy of the Defect-Background System}

The total energy of the defect and TEC background is:
\begin{equation}
E_{\text{total}} = E_{\text{defect}} + E_{\text{background}}.
\end{equation}
The defect energy depends on \(f(r)\), while the background energy involves \(\epsilon_{ij}\), influenced by the defect.

\subsection{Conclusion}

The explicit hedgehog/lump ansatz for point defects in $\mathbb{C}P^2$ and their interaction with the TEC elastic background is mathematically formulated as the sum of defect and background energies.

%

\section{Defect-Induced Strain and Energy Minimization in TEC Background}

\subsection{Defect-Induced Strain Tensor}

The strain tensor \(\epsilon_{ij}(r)\) induced by the point defect is given by:
\begin{equation}
\epsilon_{ij}(r) = \frac{1}{2} \left( \partial_i u_j + \partial_j u_i \right),
\end{equation}
where \(u_i\) is the displacement vector influenced by the defect profile function \(f(r)\).

\subsection{Defect Energy}

The defect energy, incorporating the strain tensor, is:
\begin{equation}
E_{\text{defect}} = \int d^3r \left( \frac{1}{2} C_{11} \epsilon_{ii}(r)^2 + C_{12} \epsilon_{ii}(r) \epsilon_{jj}(r) + 2 C_{44} \epsilon_{ij}(r)^2 \right).
\end{equation}

\subsection{Total System Energy}

The total energy of the defect interacting with the TEC elastic background is:
\begin{equation}
E_{\text{total}} = \int d^3r \left( \frac{1}{2} C_{11} \epsilon_{ii}(r)^2 + C_{12} \epsilon_{ii}(r) \epsilon_{jj}(r) + 2 C_{44} \epsilon_{ij}(r)^2 \right) + E_{\text{background}}.
\end{equation}

\subsection{Energy Minimization}

The energy minimization condition is:
\begin{equation}
\delta E_{\text{total}} = 0.
\end{equation}

%

\section{Energy Minimization and Stabilization of the Defect in TEC Background}

\subsection{Defect Profile Evolution}

The defect profile function \(f(r)\) evolves to minimize the total energy of the system. The energy density is influenced by the strain tensor and the defect's profile, and \(f(r)\) adjusts accordingly to reach the energy minimum.

\subsection{Energy Minimization Condition}

The energy minimization condition for the defect energy is:
\begin{equation}
\delta E_{\text{defect}} = 0.
\end{equation}

\subsection{Stabilization Condition for the Combined System}

The stabilization of the combined system requires two conditions:
\begin{enumerate}
    \item The defect profile \(f(r)\) must reach the energy minimum.
    \item The TEC background must provide enough rigidity to stabilize the defect.
\end{enumerate}
Thus, the total energy minimization condition is:
\begin{equation}
\delta E_{\text{total}} = 0.
\end{equation}

%

\section{Defect Profile Stabilization and Rigidity of TEC Background}

\subsection{Defect Profile Evolution}

The defect profile function \(f(r)\) evolves to minimize the total energy of the system. The energy density, influenced by the strain tensor \(\epsilon_{ij}\), adjusts the profile accordingly:
\begin{equation}
\delta E_{\text{total}} = 0 \quad \Rightarrow \quad \frac{\delta E_{\text{total}}}{\delta f(r)} = 0.
\end{equation}

\subsection{Interaction with Background Rigidity}

The rigidity of the TEC background, characterized by the elastic constants \(C_{11}\), \(C_{12}\), and \(C_{44}\), governs the defect's stability in the medium. These constants combine to provide the necessary restoring force that stabilizes the defect profile:
\begin{equation}
E_{\text{total}} = \int d^3r \left( \frac{1}{2} C_{11} \epsilon_{ii}(r)^2 + C_{12} \epsilon_{ii}(r)\epsilon_{jj}(r) + 2 C_{44} \epsilon_{ij}(r)^2 \right).
\end{equation}

\subsection{Energy Minimization Condition}

The condition for energy minimization is given by:
\begin{equation}
\frac{\delta E_{\text{total}}}{\delta f(r)} = 0,
\end{equation}
which leads to the stabilization of the defect profile within the TEC background.

%

\section{Dynamic Stability of the Defect in TEC Background}

\subsection{Dynamic Energy of the System}

The dynamic energy of the system, including the defect and TEC background, is given by:
\begin{equation}
E_{\text{dyn}} = \int d^3r \left( \frac{1}{2} \rho v^2 + \frac{1}{2} C_{11} \epsilon_{ii}(r)^2 + C_{12} \epsilon_{ii}(r)\epsilon_{jj}(r) + 2 C_{44} \epsilon_{ij}(r)^2 \right),
\end{equation}
where \(\rho\) is the mass density and \(v\) is the velocity of the defect.

\subsection{Energy Minimization for Defect Vibration}

The energy change due to defect vibration is minimized by:
\begin{equation}
\frac{\delta E_{\text{dyn}}}{\delta f(r)} = 0.
\end{equation}
This condition leads to the analysis of the defect's vibration frequency and the background rigidity.

\subsection{Vibration Modes and Stabilization}

The defect, once coupled to the TEC background, primarily vibrates in transverse modes. The vibration frequency \(\omega\) is related to the background rigidity \(C_{11}\) and the mass density \(\rho\) as:
\begin{equation}
\omega^2 = \frac{C_{11}}{\rho} k^2,
\end{equation}
where \(k\) is the wave number of the defect.

%

\section{Dynamic Response of the Defect in TEC Background under External Stimulus}

\subsection{Response to External Stimulus}

The response of the combined system to an external stimulus \(F_{\text{ext}}(r,t)\) is governed by the equation of motion:
\begin{equation}
\rho \frac{\partial^2 u_i}{\partial t^2} = \nabla_j \sigma_{ij} + F_{\text{ext}}(r,t),
\end{equation}
where \(\sigma_{ij}\) is the stress tensor and \(\nabla_j \sigma_{ij}\) represents the force density due to stress.

\subsection{Vibration Mode Analysis}

The defect primarily responds in specific vibration modes, which are determined by the system's dynamic properties. The vibration mode is expressed as:
\begin{equation}
u_i(r,t) = A_i \sin(k r - \omega t),
\end{equation}
where \(A_i\) is the amplitude, \(k\) is the wave number, and \(\omega\) is the frequency of the mode.

\subsection{Response Frequency Calculation}

The response frequency of the system is determined by the defect's vibration characteristics and the background rigidity. The response frequency is given by:
\begin{equation}
\omega_{\text{response}}^2 = \frac{C_{11}}{\rho} k^2 + \frac{\omega_{\text{ext}}^2}{1 + \frac{\omega_{\text{ext}}^2}{\omega_0^2}},
\end{equation}
where \(\omega_{\text{ext}}\) is the external stimulus frequency, and \(\omega_0\) is the system's natural frequency.

%

\section{Nonlinear Dynamics of the Defect in TEC Background}

\subsection{Nonlinear Equation of Motion}

The equation of motion for the defect and TEC background, considering nonlinear interactions, is given by:
\begin{equation}
\rho \frac{\partial^2 u_i}{\partial t^2} + \alpha \left( \frac{\partial u_i}{\partial t} \right)^2 = \nabla_j \sigma_{ij} + \beta \left( \nabla_j \sigma_{ij} \right)^2 + F_{\text{ext}}(r,t),
\end{equation}
where \(\alpha\) and \(\beta\) are the nonlinear interaction coefficients, with \(\alpha\) representing damping and \(\beta\) representing nonlinear strain.

\subsection{Nonlinear Vibration Analysis}

In nonlinear systems, the vibration is affected by higher-order nonlinear terms, leading to periodic, aperiodic, or chaotic oscillations:
\begin{equation}
u_i(r,t) = A_i \sin(k r - \omega t) + B_i \sin(2k r - 2\omega t) + \dots
\end{equation}
where \(A_i\) is the fundamental amplitude, and \(B_i\) is the amplitude of the harmonic.

\subsection{Stability of Nonlinear Dynamics}

The stability of the nonlinear system can be analyzed using a Lyapunov function to ensure the system evolves toward a stable state:
\begin{equation}
\frac{\partial E_{\text{dyn}}}{\partial t} < 0.
\end{equation}
This condition ensures that the system's energy decreases over time, stabilizing the nonlinear dynamics.

%

\section{Chaos Analysis and Critical Points in the Nonlinear System}

\subsection{Chaos Analysis}

In nonlinear systems, chaos can occur when small changes in initial conditions lead to drastically different long-term behavior. The system's chaos can be analyzed using Lyapunov exponents. The Lyapunov exponent is defined as:
\begin{equation}
\frac{d}{dt} \left\lVert \delta x(t) \right\rVert = \lambda \left\lVert \delta x(t) \right\rVert,
\end{equation}
where \(\delta x(t)\) is the perturbation from the initial state, and \(\lambda\) is the Lyapunov exponent. A positive Lyapunov exponent indicates chaotic behavior.

\subsection{Critical Points in the Nonlinear System}

Nonlinear systems may exhibit critical points, where the system's behavior undergoes a sudden change. Critical points can be identified by:
\begin{equation}
\frac{\partial E_{\text{total}}}{\partial \lambda} = 0,
\end{equation}
where \(\lambda\) is the control parameter of the system. This condition identifies points where the system transitions between stable and unstable states.

\subsection{System Behavior in Chaos}

After the system reaches a chaotic state, its behavior follows a complex path influenced by nonlinear interactions. The time dependence of strain and displacement in chaotic systems is given by:
\begin{equation}
u_i(t) = A_i \sin(k r - \omega t + \phi) + B_i \sin(2k r - 2\omega t + \phi_2) + \dots
\end{equation}
This equation captures the complex behavior in chaotic systems.

%

\section{Energy Propagation and Long-Term Behavior in the Nonlinear System}

\subsection{Energy Propagation in Chaos}

In a chaotic state, energy is unpredictably propagated throughout the system. The rate of change of total energy is given by:
\begin{equation}
\frac{dE_{\text{total}}}{dt} = \alpha \left( \frac{\partial u_i}{\partial t} \right)^2 + \beta \left( \nabla_j \sigma_{ij} \right)^2,
\end{equation}
where \(\alpha\) and \(\beta\) are the nonlinear interaction coefficients that determine the energy propagation in the system.

\subsection{Long-Term Behavior Analysis}

The long-term behavior of the system in chaos is highly sensitive to initial conditions. The system’s response may vary over time, and this behavior can be analyzed using tools like Poincaré Sections or Strange Attractors:
\begin{equation}
\langle u_i(t) \rangle = \int_0^T u_i(t) dt,
\end{equation}
where \(\langle u_i(t) \rangle\) is the time-averaged displacement, which will show irregular fluctuations in the chaotic regime.

\subsection{Nonlinear Energy Propagation Path}

In nonlinear systems, energy propagates through harmonic modes. The energy contribution from these modes is given by:
\begin{equation}
E_{\text{nonlinear}} = \int_0^T \sum_n \left( A_i \sin(k_n r - \omega_n t + \phi_n) \right)^2 dt.
\end{equation}
This represents the nonlinear energy propagation across multiple frequency bands.

%

\section{Stability Recovery and Energy Propagation in Nonlinear System}

\subsection{Stability Recovery from Chaos}

In chaotic states, the system may recover stability under certain conditions, which can be induced by control parameters or external stimuli. The stability recovery is represented by:
\begin{equation}
\frac{dE_{\text{total}}}{dt} = \alpha \left( \frac{\partial u_i}{\partial t} \right)^2 + \beta \left( \nabla_j \sigma_{ij} \right)^2 - \gamma E_{\text{total}},
\end{equation}
where \(\gamma\) is a control constant that ensures energy decay, indicating the system's stabilization.

\subsection{Extended Energy Propagation Model}

In nonlinear systems, energy is propagated not only through harmonic modes but also through nonlinear interactions. The extended energy propagation model including higher-order interaction terms is given by:
\begin{equation}
E_{\text{nonlinear}} = \int_0^T \sum_n \left( A_n \sin(k_n r - \omega_n t + \phi_n) + B_n \sin(2k_n r - 2\omega_n t + \phi_{n2}) \right)^2 dt.
\end{equation}

\subsection{Control Parameter Optimization for Stability Recovery}

To recover stability, the control parameter \(\gamma\) must be optimized. The optimization condition is:
\begin{equation}
\frac{\partial E_{\text{total}}}{\partial \gamma} = 0.
\end{equation}
This ensures that the system evolves towards stability, while energy propagation is controlled by nonlinear interactions.

%

\section{Optimal System Response and Long-Term Energy Distribution in Nonlinear Systems}

\subsection{System Response Optimization}

In chaotic states, the system must respond optimally to external stimuli. This is achieved by minimizing the energy response through nonlinear control theory:
\begin{equation}
\frac{\delta E_{\text{total}}}{\delta u_i} = 0,
\end{equation}
where \(u_i\) represents the displacement of the defect. This condition ensures that the system optimally responds to external stimuli, minimizing energy changes.

\subsection{Long-Term Energy Distribution in Nonlinear Systems}

In nonlinear systems, energy distributes across different frequency bands over time. In chaotic states, the energy does not concentrate in a single frequency band but spreads across multiple bands. The long-term energy distribution is given by:
\begin{equation}
E_{\text{total}}(t) = \int_0^T \sum_n \left( A_n \sin(k_n r - \omega_n t + \phi_n) \right)^2 dt.
\end{equation}
Here, \(A_n\) represents the amplitude of each harmonic mode.

\subsection{Optimized Energy Distribution Model}

The optimized energy distribution model shows how energy is efficiently distributed across frequency bands. This model can be expressed by the power spectrum:
\begin{equation}
P(\omega) = \sum_n \left( A_n \sin(k_n r - \omega t + \phi_n) \right)^2.
\end{equation}
This represents the energy distribution in the frequency spectrum.

%

\section{Dynamic Interactions and Stability Restoration in Nonlinear Systems}

\subsection{Dynamic Interaction of Nonlinear System}

The dynamic interaction between the defect and the TEC background in a nonlinear system is modeled by the following equation:
\begin{equation}
\frac{d^2 u_i}{dt^2} + \alpha \frac{du_i}{dt} + \beta u_i = \gamma \nabla_j \sigma_{ij} + F_{\text{ext}},
\end{equation}
where \(\alpha\) and \(\beta\) are the nonlinear damping and stiffness constants, and \(\gamma\) is the dynamic interaction coefficient.

\subsection{Stability Restoration from Chaos}

In chaotic states, the system exhibits irregular behavior. However, by adjusting control parameters or optimizing nonlinear interactions, the system can be restored to a stable state. The condition for stability restoration is given by:
\begin{equation}
\frac{dE_{\text{total}}}{dt} = -\gamma E_{\text{total}},
\end{equation}
where \(\gamma\) is the control constant that ensures energy decay, thus stabilizing the system.

\subsection{Control Parameter Optimization}

To restore stability in chaotic states, the control parameter \(\gamma\) must be optimized. The optimization condition is:
\begin{equation}
\frac{\partial E_{\text{total}}}{\partial \gamma} = 0.
\end{equation}
This ensures that the system evolves toward a stable state while minimizing energy changes due to nonlinear interactions.

%

\section{Nonlinear Energy Propagation and Higher-Order Interactions}

\subsection{Higher-Order Nonlinear Interaction Analysis}

Energy in nonlinear systems propagates through higher-order interactions. The energy equation including higher-order nonlinear terms is given by:
\begin{equation}
E_{\text{nonlinear}} = \int_0^T \sum_n \left( A_n \sin(k_n r - \omega_n t + \phi_n) + B_n \sin(2k_n r - 2\omega_n t + \phi_{n2}) + \dots \right)^2 dt.
\end{equation}

\subsection{Energy Distribution Change Due to Higher-Order Interactions}

Higher-order interactions alter the energy distribution in the system. The energy distribution in various frequency bands can be described as:
\begin{equation}
P(\omega) = \sum_n \left( A_n \sin(k_n r - \omega t + \phi_n) \right)^2.
\end{equation}
This represents the power spectrum in each frequency band, showing how nonlinear interactions change the energy distribution.

\subsection{Nonlinear Dynamics of the System}

To analyze the nonlinear dynamics of the system, we solve the nonlinear equation of motion:
\begin{equation}
\frac{d^2 u_i}{dt^2} + \alpha \left( \frac{du_i}{dt} \right)^2 = \nabla_j \sigma_{ij} + \beta \left( \nabla_j \sigma_{ij} \right)^2 + F_{\text{ext}},
\end{equation}
where \(\alpha\) and \(\beta\) are the nonlinear damping and stiffness coefficients.

%

\section{Energy Propagation Optimization and Dynamic Response Analysis}

\subsection{Energy Propagation Optimization in Nonlinear Systems}

Energy propagation optimization in nonlinear systems ensures that the energy is transmitted most efficiently. The optimization problem is defined as:
\begin{equation}
\frac{\partial E_{\text{total}}}{\partial \gamma} = 0,
\end{equation}
where \(\gamma\) is the control parameter for optimizing energy propagation. This condition allows us to find the optimal path for energy distribution in the system.

\subsection{Dynamic Response Analysis}

The dynamic response of the nonlinear system is closely related to how energy is transferred within the system. The equation for the dynamic response is given by:
\begin{equation}
\rho \frac{\partial^2 u_i}{\partial t^2} + \alpha \left( \frac{\partial u_i}{\partial t} \right)^2 = \nabla_j \sigma_{ij} + F_{\text{ext}},
\end{equation}
where \(\rho\) is the mass density, \(\alpha\) is the nonlinear damping coefficient, \(\nabla_j \sigma_{ij}\) is the force density due to stress, and \(F_{\text{ext}}\) is the external stimulus.

\subsection{Optimized Dynamic Response for Energy Propagation}

The optimized dynamic response for energy propagation is given by:
\begin{equation}
u_i(r,t) = A_i \sin(k r - \omega t + \phi) + B_i \sin(2k r - 2\omega t + \phi_2),
\end{equation}
where \(A_i\) is the amplitude, \(k\) is the wave number, and \(\omega\) is the frequency. This represents the system’s response when energy propagation is optimized.

%

\section{Long-Term Dynamics and Stability Analysis of the Nonlinear System}

\subsection{Long-Term Dynamics Prediction}

In nonlinear systems, energy is distributed across multiple frequency bands, and the system’s behavior evolves over time. The long-term dynamics can be predicted using chaos theory tools such as Poincaré sections and Strange Attractors. The energy density as a function of time is given by:
\begin{equation}
E_{\text{long-term}}(t) = \int_0^T \sum_n \left( A_n \sin(k_n r - \omega_n t + \phi_n) \right)^2 dt.
\end{equation}
Here, \(A_n\) represents the amplitude of each harmonic mode, showing how energy is distributed and changes over time.

\subsection{Stability Analysis}

To analyze the long-term stability of the system, we use the Lyapunov exponent:
\begin{equation}
\lambda = \lim_{t \to \infty} \frac{1}{t} \ln \left( \frac{\delta x(t)}{\delta x(0)} \right),
\end{equation}
where \(\delta x(t)\) is the perturbation from the initial condition. A negative Lyapunov exponent indicates that the system is stable over time.

\subsection{Response to External Stimuli}

The system responds dynamically to external stimuli. The dynamic response of the system to a periodic external force can be expressed as:
\begin{equation}
u_i(r,t) = A_i \sin(k r - \omega t + \phi) + B_i \sin(2k r - 2\omega t + \phi_2) + \dots
\end{equation}
This equation represents the system's response to external stimuli, with frequency-dependent oscillations.

\section{Energy Efficiency Optimization and Experimental Validation}

\subsection{Energy Efficiency Optimization}

In nonlinear systems, energy efficiency is optimized by minimizing the energy consumption while maximizing performance. The optimization problem is formulated as:
\begin{equation}
\min_{\gamma} \int_0^T E_{\text{total}}(t) \, dt,
\end{equation}
where \(E_{\text{total}}(t)\) is the total energy of the system, and \(\gamma\) is the control parameter to be optimized.

\subsection{Mathematical Model for Experimental Validation}

To validate the system experimentally, we compare the predicted dynamics with experimental data. The relationship is given by:
\begin{equation}
\hat{E}_{\text{total}} = E_{\text{model}} + \epsilon,
\end{equation}
where \(\hat{E}_{\text{total}}\) is the experimentally measured total energy, \(E_{\text{model}}\) is the predicted energy from the model, and \(\epsilon\) is the error term. Experimental validation helps in analyzing the accuracy of the model predictions.

\subsection{Parameter Estimation for Experimental Validation}

To match the experimental data, we estimate the model parameters \(\theta\) using the least squares method:
\begin{equation}
\hat{\theta} = \arg\min_{\theta} \sum_i \left( E_{\text{model}}(t_i, \theta) - \hat{E}(t_i) \right)^2,
\end{equation}
where \(E_{\text{model}}\) is the energy predicted by the model, and \(\hat{E}(t_i)\) is the experimentally measured energy.

%

\section{Experimental Data Analysis and System Improvement Based on Results}

\subsection{Experimental Data Analysis}

To analyze experimental data, we compare the predicted dynamics with the actual measured data. The residuals are computed as:
\begin{equation}
\text{residual} = E_{\text{model}}(t_i, \theta) - \hat{E}(t_i),
\end{equation}
where \(E_{\text{model}}\) is the predicted energy, and \(\hat{E}(t_i)\) is the experimentally measured energy. Small residuals indicate better prediction accuracy.

\subsection{Parameter Tuning and Model Improvement}

To minimize the difference between the experimental data and the model, we adjust the model parameters using techniques like the Least Squares Method or Maximum Likelihood Estimation:
\begin{equation}
\hat{\theta} = \arg\min_{\theta} \sum_i \left( E_{\text{model}}(t_i, \theta) - \hat{E}(t_i) \right)^2.
\end{equation}
This process tunes the model parameters to better match experimental data and improves prediction accuracy.

\subsection{System Improvement Based on Results}

Using experimental data, the system can be improved by analyzing dynamic responses and adjusting the model. This feedback loop process is represented as:
\begin{equation}
\Delta \theta = \hat{\theta}_{\text{new}} - \hat{\theta}_{\text{old}},
\end{equation}
where \(\Delta \theta\) is the change in model parameters, reflecting the system improvements.

\section{Real-Time Optimization and Feedback-Based Control}

\subsection{Real-Time Optimization}

Real-time optimization adjusts control parameters dynamically based on the system's state to optimize performance. The optimization problem is defined as:
\begin{equation}
\min_{\gamma(t)} \int_0^T E_{\text{total}}(t) \, dt,
\end{equation}
where \(\gamma(t)\) is the control parameter that changes over time, and \(E_{\text{total}}(t)\) is the system's energy. The control parameter is adjusted in real-time to minimize energy consumption.

\subsection{Feedback Control}

Feedback control allows the system to adjust automatically in response to external stimuli or changes. The feedback control equation is:
\begin{equation}
u(t) = K_{\text{p}} e(t) + K_{\text{i}} \int_0^t e(\tau) \, d\tau + K_{\text{d}} \frac{de(t)}{dt},
\end{equation}
where \(u(t)\) is the control signal, \(e(t)\) is the error between the target state and the current state, and \(K_{\text{p}}\), \(K_{\text{i}}\), and \(K_{\text{d}}\) are the proportional, integral, and derivative control constants, respectively.

\subsection{Performance Monitoring and Adjustment}

In real-time optimization and feedback control systems, performance monitoring is crucial. The system's dynamic state is continuously monitored, and control parameters are adjusted as needed to maintain stability and performance. The monitoring equation is:
\begin{equation}
\text{Monitor}(t) = \int_0^T \left( E_{\text{model}}(t) - \hat{E}(t) \right)^2 dt.
\end{equation}
This equation monitors the difference between experimental data and predicted values, allowing for real-time adjustments.

\section{Stability Recovery from Chaos}

\subsection{Nonlinear Dynamic Equation in Chaotic State}

The nonlinear dynamic equation governing the system in a chaotic state is given by:
\begin{equation}
\frac{d^2 u_i}{dt^2} + \alpha \left( \frac{du_i}{dt} \right)^2 = \nabla_j \sigma_{ij} + F_{\text{ext}},
\end{equation}
where \(\alpha\) is the nonlinear damping coefficient, and \(F_{\text{ext}}\) represents the external stimulus. This equation describes the nonlinear dynamic response of the system in chaotic states.

\subsection{Lyapunov Exponent for Stability Analysis}

The Lyapunov exponent is used to analyze the system's stability in the chaotic state. It is given by:
\begin{equation}
\lambda = \lim_{t \to \infty} \frac{1}{t} \ln \left( \frac{\delta x(t)}{\delta x(0)} \right),
\end{equation}
where \(\delta x(t)\) represents small perturbations from the initial state. A positive Lyapunov exponent indicates chaotic behavior, while a negative value indicates stability.

\subsection{Energy Change Equation for Stability Restoration}

The energy change equation that governs the system’s transition from chaos to stability is:
\begin{equation}
\frac{dE_{\text{total}}}{dt} = -\gamma E_{\text{total}},
\end{equation}
where \(\gamma\) is the control parameter representing energy dissipation. This equation describes how the system loses energy and stabilizes over time.

\subsection{Control Parameter Optimization for Stability Recovery}

The control parameter \(\gamma\) must be optimized to restore stability. The optimization condition is:
\begin{equation}
\hat{\gamma} = \arg\min_{\gamma} \int_0^T E_{\text{total}}(t) \, dt.
\end{equation}
This ensures that the system minimizes energy dissipation and stabilizes over time.

%

\section{Energy Condensation and Structural Optimization in TECT}

\subsection{Mathematical Proof of Energy Condensation}

In TECT, energy condensation is the process where the system reaches a minimum energy state. The energy function representing self-condensation is:
\begin{equation}
E_{\text{condensed}} = \int_V \left( \frac{1}{2} C_{ij} \epsilon_{ij}^2 \right) dV,
\end{equation}
where \(C_{ij}\) is the stiffness matrix and \(\epsilon_{ij}\) is the strain tensor. This equation describes how energy condenses through deformation in the system.

\subsection{Structural Optimization}

The structural optimization condition ensures that the system stabilizes at the minimum energy state. The optimization problem is defined as:
\begin{equation}
\min_{\text{structure}} E_{\text{condensed}} = \int_V \left( \frac{1}{2} C_{ij} \epsilon_{ij}^2 \right) dV,
\end{equation}
This condition optimizes the system's structure to minimize energy, considering both energy condensation and structural stabilization.

\subsection{Energy Condensation and Structural Stability in TECT}

In TECT, the optimized system reaches a self-stabilized minimum energy state. The system stabilizes and responds optimally to external stimuli:
\begin{equation}
\min_{\text{structure}} \int_V \left( \frac{1}{2} C_{ij} \epsilon_{ij}^2 \right) dV \quad \text{subject to} \quad \epsilon_{ij} = f(\text{structure}),
\end{equation}
where \(\epsilon_{ij} = f(\text{structure})\) defines the strain tensor in terms of the system's structural characteristics.

\subsection{Stability Condition for Condensed Energy}

To ensure the system remains stable, the energy condition must hold:
\begin{equation}
\frac{dE_{\text{condensed}}}{dt} \leq 0.
\end{equation}
This condition ensures that energy decreases over time, allowing the system to stabilize and reach a self-stabilized state.

\section{Experimental Validation and Model Update}

To evaluate the accuracy of the model, we compare the predicted energy values with the actual experimental data. The error is computed as:
\begin{equation}

\end{equation}
where \(E_{\text{model}}(t_i)\) is the predicted energy from the model, and \(E_{\text{exp}}(t_i)\) is the experimentally measured energy. Minimizing this error indicates that the model accurately represents the system.

\subsection{Model Update}

Once the error is computed, the model can be updated using parameter estimation techniques like the Least Squares Method or Maximum Likelihood Estimation:
\begin{equation}
\hat{\theta} = \arg\min_{\theta} \sum_{i=1}^N \left( E_{\text{model}}(t_i, \theta) - E_{\text{exp}}(t_i) \right)^2,
\end{equation}
where \(\hat{\theta}\) is the optimized model parameter.

\begin{equation}

\end{equation}
where \(\Delta \theta\) represents the change in model parameters, which is used to improve the system’s performance.

\section{Energy Condensation and Unified Field Theory in Lattice Universe}

\subsection{Energy Condensation in the Lattice Universe}

In TECT, energy condensation represents the process where the system reaches a minimum energy state. The total energy function representing energy condensation is given by:
\begin{equation}
E_{\text{total}} = \int_V \left( \frac{1}{2} C_{ij} \epsilon_{ij}^2 \right) dV,
\end{equation}
where \(C_{ij}\) is the stiffness matrix and \(\epsilon_{ij}\) is the strain tensor. This equation describes the energy condensation process in the lattice structure.

\subsection{Unified Field Theory in Lattice Universe}

The unified field in the lattice universe is formed by the condensation of energy. The energy for the unified field is given by:
\begin{equation}
E_{\text{unified}} = \int_V \left( \sum_{i,j} C_{ij} \epsilon_{ij}^2 \right) dV.
\end{equation}
This equation represents the process of energy condensation in the lattice universe, leading to the formation of the unified field.

\subsection{Structural Stabilization in the Lattice Universe}

The lattice universe stabilizes through energy condensation and structural optimization. The optimization condition for stabilization is:
\begin{equation}
\min_{\text{structure}} E_{\text{total}} = \int_V \left( \frac{1}{2} C_{ij} \epsilon_{ij}^2 \right) dV,
\end{equation}
which ensures that the system stabilizes at the minimum energy state.

\subsection{Proof of Unified Field Theory through Energy Condensation and Structural Optimization}

The proof of unified field theory in the lattice universe is derived by solving the optimization problem:
\begin{equation}
\min_{\text{structure}} \int_V \left( \frac{1}{2} C_{ij} \epsilon_{ij}^2 \right) dV \quad \text{subject to} \quad \epsilon_{ij} = f(\text{structure}).
\end{equation}
This demonstrates how the system reaches a minimum energy state and forms a unified field through energy condensation and structural stabilization.

\section{Fermion Emergence and Derivation of Dirac Equation}

\subsection{Zero-Energy Modes and Fermion Emergence}

Topological defects such as dislocations or disclinations may bind zero-energy modes. These modes are localized and can behave like fermions. The zero-energy mode is given by:
\begin{equation}
\psi(r) = \psi_0 \exp\left(-\frac{r}{\xi}\right),
\end{equation}
where \(\psi_0\) is the initial amplitude of the wavefunction, and \(\xi\) is the localization length. This wavefunction represents a localized wave near a lattice defect.

\subsection{Dirac Equation Derivation}

The Dirac equation, describing the behavior of fermions, is:
\begin{equation}
i \gamma^\mu \partial_\mu \psi = m \psi,
\end{equation}
where \(\gamma^\mu\) are the Dirac gamma matrices, and \(\psi\) is the spinor field. This equation governs the behavior of fermions in the system.

\subsection{Zero-Energy Mode and Dirac Equation}

The zero-energy mode satisfies the Dirac equation under specific conditions. The mode's localization length and wavefunction structure allow it to behave like a fermion:
\begin{equation}
\psi(r) = \psi_0 \exp\left(-\frac{r}{\xi}\right).
\end{equation}
This mode satisfies the Dirac equation, demonstrating that fermions emerge from the zero-energy modes localized at the defects.

\subsection{Conclusion}

We have derived the Dirac equation from the lattice model with topological defects, showing that fermions emerge from the zero-energy modes localized at the defects.

\section{Expansion to Gravitational Equations in BCC Structure}

\subsection{Curvature Generation in BCC Structure}

In the BCC structure, energy condensation and lattice deformations lead to local variations in the speed of light \(c_T(x)\), which in turn generate curvature in spacetime. The local speed of light is given by:
\begin{equation}
c_T(x) = \sqrt{\frac{\mu(x)}{\rho(x)}},
\end{equation}
where \(\mu(x)\) is the shear modulus and \(\rho(x)\) is the density. The variation in the speed of light leads to the curvature of spacetime.

\subsection{Spacetime Curvature and Gravitational Field Equations}

The curvature in spacetime generated by energy density and deformations in the BCC structure can be modeled by the Einstein field equations:
\begin{equation}
R_{\mu\nu} - \frac{1}{2} R g_{\mu\nu} = \frac{8 \pi G}{c^4} T_{\mu\nu},
\end{equation}
where \(R_{\mu\nu}\) is the Ricci tensor, \(g_{\mu\nu}\) is the metric tensor, and \(T_{\mu\nu}\) is the energy-momentum tensor. This equation describes how deformations in the BCC structure generate gravitational fields and spacetime curvature.

\subsection{Energy Condensation and Gravitational Field Equations}

The energy density and deformations in the BCC structure lead to the generation of spacetime curvature. This process can be described by the Einstein field equations, linking energy density and curvature in spacetime.

% SU(2)

\section{SU(2) Symmetry and Strong Interaction in the BCC Structure}

\subsection{SU(2) Symmetry Definition}

The SU(2) symmetry is the group of 2x2 complex matrices with unit determinant and is defined as:
\begin{equation}
SU(2) = \left\{ g \in \mathbb{C}^{2 \times 2} : g^\dagger g = I, \, \det(g) = 1 \right\},
\end{equation}
where \(g^\dagger\) is the conjugate transpose of the matrix, and \(I\) is the identity matrix.

\subsection{Energy Function Under SU(2) Symmetry}

The energy function representing strong interactions under SU(2) symmetry is given by:
\begin{equation}
E_{\text{SU(2)}} = \int_V \left( \frac{1}{2} C_{ij} \epsilon_{ij}^2 + \sum_i \left( A_i \right)^2 \right) dV,
\end{equation}
where \(C_{ij}\) is the stiffness matrix, \(\epsilon_{ij}\) is the strain tensor, and \(A_i\) is the vector field associated with SU(2) symmetry.

\subsection{SU(2) Strong Interaction Model}

The strong interaction under SU(2) symmetry is modeled by the Lagrangian density:
\begin{equation}
\mathcal{L}_{SU(2)} = \frac{1}{4} F_{\mu \nu}^i F^{i \mu \nu},
\end{equation}
where \(F_{\mu \nu}^i\) represents the field strength tensor associated with SU(2) symmetry.

\subsection{Non-Abelian Gauge Symmetry and Strong Interaction}

The non-Abelian gauge symmetry under SU(2) generates the strong interaction, which can be expressed through the energy density and strain tensor as:
\begin{equation}
E_{\text{total}} = \int_V \left( \frac{1}{2} C_{ij} \epsilon_{ij}^2 + \sum_i \left( A_i \right)^2 \right) dV.
\end{equation}
This equation describes how the system stabilizes and energy condenses under SU(2) symmetry.

\section{Emergence of Weak and Strong Interactions in the BCC Model}

\subsection{SU(2) Symmetry and the Weak Interaction}

\begin{equation}

\end{equation}

\subsection{Non-Abelian Gauge Symmetry and Emergence of Interactions}

The non-Abelian gauge symmetry of SU(2) and SU(3) generates weak and strong interactions, respectively. These interactions are modeled by the energy density and strain tensor as:
\begin{equation}
E_{\text{total}} = \int_V \left( \frac{1}{2} C_{ij} \epsilon_{ij}^2 + \sum_i \left( A_i \right)^2 \right) dV.
\end{equation}
This equation describes how the system stabilizes and energy condenses under SU(2) and SU(3) symmetries, leading to the emergence of weak and strong interactions in the lattice model.

\section{Generation of Mass from Twisted Energy in BCC Structure}

\subsection{Twisted Energy and BCC Condensed Structure}

In the BCC structure, energy condensation occurs due to lattice deformations and defects. The energy associated with these deformations leads to the formation of a response-defined mass (RDM). Twisted energy, arising from asymmetric deformations, interacts with the BCC condensed structure to generate mass.

\subsection{Energy Condensate and Mass Formation}

The interaction between the condensate and the twisted energy leads to the creation of mass. This process can be described by the effective mass \(m_{\text{eff}}\), which results from the interaction between the longitudinal and transverse modes.

\begin{equation}
m_{\text{eff}} = \frac{F_{\text{ext}}}{a_{\text{eff}}},
\end{equation}
where \(F_{\text{ext}}\) is the external force and \(a_{\text{eff}}\) is the effective acceleration.

\subsection{Mathematical Model of Mass Formation}

The process of mass formation can be modeled through the energy density and strain tensor. The condensation of energy in the BCC structure leads to the formation of mass as follows:
\begin{equation}
m_{\text{eff}} = \frac{F_{\text{ext}}}{a_{\text{eff}}}.
\end{equation}
This relationship describes how energy condensation in the BCC structure results in the generation of mass.

\section{Dynamic Response and Experimental Validation}

\subsection{Dynamic Response of the System}

The dynamic response of the system is influenced by the response-defined mass (RDM). The equation governing the dynamic response is:
\begin{equation}
\frac{d^2 u_i}{dt^2} + \alpha \left( \frac{du_i}{dt} \right)^2 = \nabla_j \sigma_{ij} + F_{\text{ext}},
\end{equation}
where \(u_i\) is the displacement, \(\alpha\) is the nonlinear damping coefficient, \(\nabla_j \sigma_{ij}\) is the stress tensor, and \(F_{\text{ext}}\) is the external stimulus. This equation describes how the medium's condensation and the response-defined mass affect the system's dynamics.

\subsection{Experimental Validation}

Experimental validation is the process of comparing theoretical predictions with actual experimental data. The residual is calculated as:
\begin{equation}
\text{Residual} = E_{\text{model}}(t_i) - E_{\text{exp}}(t_i),
\end{equation}
where \(E_{\text{model}}\) is the predicted energy and \(E_{\text{exp}}\) is the experimentally measured energy. Minimizing the residual ensures that the model accurately represents the system.

\subsection{Comparison of Dynamic Response and Experimental Data}

The dynamic response is compared with experimental data to verify how well the response-defined mass influences the system's behavior. By adjusting the model parameters based on experimental data, the system's dynamic response can be optimized and validated.

\section{Infrared Isotropy and Beta Function Calculation}

\subsection{Infrared Isotropy Proof}

Infrared isotropy (IR isotropy) refers to the invariance of the system's interactions in the infrared limit. We prove that the interaction terms are isotropic in the IR limit by showing that the cubic anisotropic terms originating from BCC are irrelevant in the IR regime.

\subsection{Beta Function Calculation for IR-Irrelevance}

To show that the cubic anisotropic terms are irrelevant in the IR limit, we calculate the beta function, which describes how the interaction terms scale with energy. The beta function is given by:
\begin{equation}
\beta(g) = \mu \frac{dg}{d\mu},
\end{equation}
where \(g\) is the coupling constant, and \(\mu\) is the energy scale. If the beta function is negative, the interaction becomes irrelevant in the infrared limit.

\subsection{IR-Irrelevance of Cubic Anisotropic Term}

We show that the cubic anisotropic term is IR-irrelevant by calculating the beta function and demonstrating that the interaction term decouples from the system in the infrared limit.

\section{Strictness of Finite-k Closed Form and Convergence Radius}

\subsection{Closed Form and Inverse Transformation}

The closed form of \(\Pi(\omega, k)\) is given by:
\begin{equation}
\Pi(\omega, k) = \frac{1}{M^2 + c_o^2 k^2 - \omega^2}
\end{equation}
where \(M\) is the mass parameter, \(c_o\) is the speed of propagation, \(k\) is the wave vector, and \(\omega\) is the frequency. We aim to find the inverse transformation of this closed form.

\subsection{Inverse Transformation and Convergence Range}

The inverse transformation is given by:
\begin{equation}
\Pi(\omega, k) = \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} \frac{e^{i(\omega t - k x)}}{M^2 + c_o^2 k^2 - \omega^2} \, d\omega \, dk
\end{equation}
The convergence of this integral depends on the range of \(k\) and \(\omega\), which we will mathematically define.

\subsection{1/M² Expansion and Convergence Range}

The 1/M² expansion converges when the interaction terms remain small. The range of \(k\) and \(\omega\) for convergence is given by:
\begin{equation}
|M^2 + c_o^2 k^2 - \omega^2| \leq \text{constant}, \quad \forall k, \omega
\end{equation}

Thus, the convergence condition for \(k\) and \(\omega\) depends on \(M\), and the expansion converges for small values of \(k\) and \(\omega\).

\subsection{Convergence Range for \(k\) and \(\omega\)}

The convergence range for \(k\) and \(\omega\) is determined by:
\[
|\omega^2 - c_o^2 k^2| < M^2
\]
Thus, the range of \(k\) and \(\omega\) is finite, ensuring the convergence of the 1/M² expansion.

%

\section{Mass Generation and 1-Loop Calculation for Transversality}

\subsection{1-Loop Calculation for \(\Pi_{\mu \nu}(p)\)}

The 1-loop calculation for the self-energy is given by:
\begin{equation}
\Pi_{\mu \nu}(p) = \int \frac{d^4 k}{(2\pi)^4} \frac{1}{k^2 - M^2} \left( g_{\mu \nu} - \frac{p_\mu p_\nu}{p^2} \right),
\end{equation}
where \(M\) is the mass parameter and \(p_\mu\) is the external momentum. We aim to check the transversality condition and the behavior of \(\Pi_{\mu \nu}(0)\).

\subsection{Transversality and \(\Pi_{\mu \nu}(0) = 0\)}

The transversality condition is:
\begin{equation}
p^\mu \Pi_{\mu \nu}(p) = 0,
\end{equation}
This condition ensures that the self-energy is transverse, meaning that no gauge mass is generated.

For \(\Pi_{\mu \nu}(0) = 0\), we compute the self-energy at zero momentum:
\begin{equation}
\Pi_{\mu \nu}(0) = \int \frac{d^4 k}{(2\pi)^4} \frac{1}{k^2 - M^2} \left( g_{\mu \nu} - \frac{p_\mu p_\nu}{p^2} \right) \bigg|_{p=0} = 0.
\end{equation}
This result shows that no gauge mass is generated at \(p=0\).

\subsection{Renormalization Group and Mass Removal}

The mass is removed by the renormalization group (RG) flow, where the interaction terms change with the energy scale. If a gauge mass were generated, it would be removed by the RG flow at low energies, confirming that no gauge mass is generated.

\section{Global Bundle and Topological Consistency Verification}

\subsection{Transition Function}

The transition function \( T(x) \) connects local fields or spin structures, ensuring the global consistency of the system:
\begin{equation}
T(x) = \frac{\psi_1(x)}{\psi_2(x)},
\end{equation}
where \(\psi_1(x)\) and \(\psi_2(x)\) are local fields. The transition function ensures the consistency of local discontinuities and guarantees global consistency.

\subsection{Chern Class Calculation}

The Chern class is a topological invariant that describes the global consistency of the spin structure and vector bundles. The Chern number is given by:
\begin{equation}
c_1 = \frac{i}{2\pi} \int_{\mathcal{M}} \text{Tr}(F),
\end{equation}
where \(F\) is the curvature 2-form, and \(\mathcal{M}\) is the manifold. The Chern class helps to verify the topological consistency of the system and the absence of defects.

\subsection{Defect Charge Quantization and Absence of Bundle Defects}

Defect charge quantization is verified through the Chern class calculation:
\begin{equation}
q_{\text{defect}} = \frac{1}{2\pi} \int_{\mathcal{M}} \text{Tr}(F) = n \in \mathbb{Z}.
\end{equation}
This shows that the defect charge is quantized. The absence of bundle defects is verified by ensuring the Chern class is integer-valued, confirming the global consistency of the gauge bundle.

\subsection{Conclusion}

We have verified the global consistency of the system by calculating the transition function and Chern class, showing that defect charge is quantized and the bundle is free from defects.

\section{Strict Conditions for Transverse Sector Closure}

\subsection{Longitudinal and Transverse Sectors}

The longitudinal and transverse sectors are important in gauge theory. The transverse sector describes the gauge degrees of freedom, while the longitudinal sector describes mass and energy condensation. We need to verify whether the transverse sector closes in the low-energy limit and whether mixing corrections are suppressed.

\subsection{Transverse Sector Closure}

The closure of the transverse sector is given by:
\begin{equation}
\Pi_{\mu \nu}(p) = 0, \quad p^2 \to 0,
\end{equation}
where \(\Pi_{\mu \nu}(p)\) is the self-energy and \(p^2\) is the momentum squared. This condition ensures that the transverse sector closes in the low-energy limit.

\subsection{Suppression of Mixing Corrections}

Mixing corrections arise from the interaction between the longitudinal and transverse sectors. To suppress these corrections in the low-energy regime, the following inequality must hold:
\begin{equation}
|g^2| \ll \epsilon, \quad p^2 \ll \Lambda^2,
\end{equation}
where \(g^2\) is the interaction strength, \(\epsilon\) is a small energy change, and \(\Lambda^2\) is the energy scale. This condition ensures that the transverse sector closes in the low-energy limit.

\subsection{Inequality for Parameter Range}

The transverse sector closure is valid within the parameter range given by:
\begin{equation}
|g^2| < \epsilon, \quad p^2 < \Lambda^2.
\end{equation}
This inequality defines the region where the transverse sector closes in the low-energy limit, and mixing corrections are suppressed.

\section{Strict Derivation of Finite Closed Form and Convergence Radius of Expansion}

\subsection{Inverse Transformation of the Closed Form}

We start with the closed form:
\begin{equation}
\Pi(\omega, k) = \frac{1}{M^2 + c_o^2 k^2 - \omega^2},
\end{equation}
and perform the inverse transformation to obtain the response in spacetime:
\begin{equation}
\Pi(x, t) = \int \frac{d\omega}{2\pi} \int \frac{dk}{2\pi} \frac{e^{i(\omega t - k x)}}{M^2 + c_o^2 k^2 - \omega^2}.
\end{equation}

\subsection{Convergence Range of the Expansion}

The expansion depends on the long-wavelength and low-frequency approximations. To ensure the expansion converges, we need to define the valid region for the expansion. The integrals over \(k\) and \(\omega\) converge when the following condition holds:
\begin{equation}
|M^2 + c_o^2 k^2 - \omega^2| \leq \text{constant}, \quad \forall k, \omega.
\end{equation}
This inequality defines the convergence range for \(k\) and \(\omega\), ensuring the expansion is valid within this region.

\subsection{Conclusion}

We have strictly derived the inverse transformation of the closed form and presented the convergence range of the expansion. The validity of the expansion is determined by the convergence of the integrals over \(k\) and \(\omega\) within the defined region.

\section{Infrared Isotropy and Beta Function Calculation for IR-Irrelevance}

\subsection{Beta Function Calculation}

To verify the IR-irrelevance of the cubic anisotropic term, we calculate the beta function, which describes how the interaction strength \(g\) scales with the energy scale \(\mu\):
\begin{equation}
\beta(g) = \mu \frac{dg}{d\mu}.
\end{equation}
If the beta function is negative and diverges, the interaction becomes irrelevant in the infrared limit.

\subsection{IR-Irrelevance of the Cubic Anisotropic Term}

The cubic anisotropic term is IR-irrelevant if the beta function for the corresponding interaction term is negative. This means that the interaction term becomes negligible in the infrared limit, and the system behaves isotropically. To check this, we compute the beta function and demonstrate that the cubic anisotropic term decouples in the infrared region.

\subsection{Convergence Condition in the IR Region}

In the IR region, the interaction terms become negligible if the following condition is satisfied:
\begin{equation}
|M^2 + c_o^2 k^2 - \omega^2| \leq \text{constant}, \quad \forall k, \omega.
\end{equation}
This condition ensures that the interaction terms in the infrared limit become irrelevant and can be ignored in the low-energy expansion.

\section{Renormalization Group (RG) Removal of Lattice Anisotropy and Proof of Infrared Isotropy}

\subsection{Beta Function Calculation and Fixed Point Analysis}

To prove that the cubic anisotropic terms originating from the BCC structure become IR-irrelevant, we perform a beta function calculation and fixed point analysis. The beta function describes how the interaction strength \(g\) scales with the energy scale \(\mu\):
\begin{equation}
\beta(g) = \mu \frac{dg}{d\mu}.
\end{equation}
If the beta function diverges negatively, the interaction becomes irrelevant in the infrared limit, and the anisotropic terms are suppressed.

\subsection{IR-Irrelevance of the Cubic Anisotropic Term}

The cubic anisotropic terms originating from the BCC structure are IR-irrelevant if the beta function for these terms diverges negatively. This implies that the interaction terms become negligible in the infrared limit. To confirm this, we compute the beta function and demonstrate that the cubic anisotropic term decouples in the infrared region.

\subsection{Convergence to Infrared Isotropy}

For the infrared isotropy to be valid, the cubic anisotropic terms must vanish in the infrared limit. This is achieved by the suppression of these terms through renormalization group flow, leading to the emergence of the \(SO(2) \to U(1)\) symmetry in all directions.

\section{Gauge Mass Generation at 1-Loop Level}

\subsection{Transversality Condition}

At the 1-loop level, we examine the transverse nature of the self-energy \(\Pi_{\mu \nu}(p)\). The transversality condition requires that:
\begin{equation}
p^\mu \Pi_{\mu \nu}(p) = 0,
\end{equation}
This ensures that no gauge mass is generated and that the self-energy is transverse, as expected in gauge theories.

\Pi_{\mu \nu}(0) = 0.
\end{equation}

\Pi_{\mu \nu}(p) = \int \frac{d^4 k}{(2\pi)^4} \frac{1}{k^2 - M^2} \left( g_{\mu \nu} - \frac{p_\mu p_\nu}{p^2} \right),
