\section{Appendix A: Finite-\(k\) Closed Form from Integrating Out the Volumetric Mode and the \(1/M^2\) Expansion}


\subsection{abstract}
This appendix derives the finite-\(k\) closed form kernel obtained by integrating out the gapped volumetric mode \(o\) in the TECT effective theory, presents the inverse Fourier transform (spacetime kernel), and gives the systematic \(1/M^2\) expansion together with explicit remainder bounds and the expansion's domain of validity. The goal is to make precise the statements used in the main text about nonlocal corrections and the conditions under which a local \(1/M^2\) expansion is justified.


\subsection{Starting point: relevant quadratic sector}
We consider the low-energy quadratic sector coupling the volumetric scalar \(o\) to the longitudinal displacement \(u_L\). In Fourier space (frequency \(\omega\), wavevector \(\mathbf{k}\)) the relevant Lagrangian density is taken as
\begin{equation}
\mathcal{L}(\omega,\mathbf{k})
= \tfrac{1}{2}\big(-\omega^2 + c_o^2 k^2 + M^2\big)\,|o(\omega,\mathbf{k})|^2
+ \tfrac{1}{2}\rho\big(-\omega^2 + v_L^2 k^2\big)\,|u_L(\omega,\mathbf{k})|^2
+ \big[\,g(\mathbf{k})\,(i k)\, o(-\omega,-\mathbf{k})\, u_L(\omega,\mathbf{k}) + \mathrm{c.c.}\,\big],
\label{eq:starting}
\end{equation}
where \(k=|\mathbf{k}|\), \(u_L=\hat{k}\cdot\mathbf{u}\) is the longitudinal component, \(M\) is the volumetric gap, \(c_o\) its characteristic velocity, and \(g(\mathbf{k})\) the coupling form factor (assumed smooth at small \(k\)).

\subsection{Gaussian integration of the volumetric mode}
Because the action is quadratic in \(o\), integrating out \(o\) yields an exact closed-form correction to the effective action for \(u_L\). Denote the volumetric propagator by
\[
G_o(\omega,k) \;=\; \frac{1}{M^2 + c_o^2 k^2 - \omega^2 + i0^+}.
\]
Then the induced effective action correction is
\begin{equation}
\Delta S_{\mathrm{eff}}[u_L]
= -\frac{1}{2}\int\frac{d\omega}{2\pi}\frac{d^3k}{(2\pi)^3}\; |g(\mathbf{k})|^2 k^2\;
\frac{|u_L(\omega,\mathbf{k})|^2}{M^2 + c_o^2 k^2 - \omega^2 + i0^+}.
\label{eq:DeltaS}
\end{equation}
Define the polarization kernel
\begin{equation}
\Pi(\omega,k) \;:=\; \frac{|g(\mathbf{k})|^2 k^2}{M^2 + c_o^2 k^2 - \omega^2 + i0^+},
\label{eq:PiDef}
\end{equation}
so that \(\Delta S_{\mathrm{eff}} = -\tfrac12\!\int\!\frac{d\omega d^3k}{(2\pi)^4}\, \Pi(\omega,k)\,|u_L|^2\).

\subsection{Exact static kernel and nonlocality}
For the static response (\(\omega=0\)) the exact \(k\)-dependent correction to the longitudinal stiffness is
\begin{equation}
\delta\mathcal{K}_L(k) \;=\; -\frac{|g(\mathbf{k})|^2 k^2}{M^2 + c_o^2 k^2}.
\label{eq:deltaKstatic}
\end{equation}
This expression is manifestly nonlocal in real space: its Fourier transform produces higher-derivative operators whose coefficients depend on the ratio \(c_o k/M\). The nonlocality becomes significant when \(c_o k \sim M\).

\subsection{Low-energy expansion: \(1/M^2\) series}
Assume the low-energy regime
\begin{equation}
c_o^2 k^2 \ll M^2,\qquad \omega^2 \ll M^2,
\label{eq:lowenergy}
\end{equation}
so that the denominator in \eqref{eq:PiDef} can be expanded as a convergent geometric series:
\begin{equation}
\frac{1}{M^2 + c_o^2 k^2 - \omega^2}
= \frac{1}{M^2}\sum_{n=0}^\infty \Big(-\frac{c_o^2 k^2 - \omega^2}{M^2}\Big)^n.
\label{eq:geom}
\end{equation}
Hence
\begin{equation}
\Pi(\omega,k) = \frac{|g(\mathbf{k})|^2 k^2}{M^2}\left[1 - \frac{c_o^2 k^2 - \omega^2}{M^2} + \mathcal{O}\!\big(M^{-4}\big)\right].
\label{eq:pi_expand}
\end{equation}
Truncating at \(O(M^{-4})\) and transforming back to real space yields local higher-derivative corrections:
\begin{align}
\Delta\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{eff}}(t,\mathbf{x})
&= -\frac{|g|^2}{2M^2}\,(\nabla u_L)^2
+\frac{|g|^2 c_o^2}{2M^4}\,(\nabla^2 u_L)^2
-\frac{|g|^2}{2M^4}\,(\partial_t \nabla u_L)^2
+\mathcal{O}\!\big(M^{-6}\big),
\label{eq:localexpansion}
\end{align}
where \(g:=g(\mathbf{k}\to0)\) denotes the small-\(k\) limit of the coupling.

\subsection{Remainder estimate and radius of convergence}
Let \(R_N(\omega,k)\) denote the remainder after truncating the geometric series at order \(N\). For \(|c_o^2 k^2 - \omega^2| < M^2\) the standard geometric-series remainder bound gives
\begin{equation}
\big|R_N(\omega,k)\big|
\le \frac{|g(\mathbf{k})|^2 k^2}{M^2}\cdot
\frac{\big|c_o^2 k^2 - \omega^2\big|^{\,N+1}}{M^{2(N+1)}\big(1 - \frac{|c_o^2 k^2 - \omega^2|}{M^2}\big)}.
\label{eq:remainder}
\end{equation}
Therefore the \(1/M^2\) expansion converges uniformly on any compact subset of the domain
\[
\mathcal{D} = \{(\omega,k)\in\mathbb{R}\times\mathbb{R}^+ \;:\; |c_o^2 k^2 - \omega^2| < M^2\}.
\]
In particular, the commonly used sufficient condition \(\,c_o^2 k^2 \ll M^2,\ \omega^2\ll M^2\,\) ensures rapid convergence.

\subsection{Spacetime kernel: inverse Fourier transform}
The exact spacetime kernel \(K(t,\mathbf{x})\) associated with \(\Pi(\omega,k)\) is defined by
\[
K(t,\mathbf{x}) \;=\; \int\frac{d\omega}{2\pi}\frac{d^3k}{(2\pi)^3}\; e^{i(\mathbf{k}\cdot\mathbf{x}-\omega t)}\;
\frac{|g(\mathbf{k})|^2 k^2}{M^2 + c_o^2 k^2 - \omega^2 + i0^+}.
\]
For analytic estimates one may perform the \(\omega\)-integral by contour methods, yielding retarded/advanced Green's function representations. In the static limit \(t=0\) the spatial kernel reduces to the Fourier transform of \eqref{eq:deltaKstatic}. For practical purposes one often evaluates \(K\) numerically for representative parameter sets; the local expansion \eqref{eq:localexpansion} provides an analytic approximation in the regime \eqref{eq:lowenergy}.

\subsection{Impact on the transverse sector and gauge field}
\begin{itemize}
  \item \textbf{Direct coupling:} At quadratic order the volumetric mode couples only to the longitudinal component \(u_L\); therefore integrating out \(o\) does not directly produce a mass term for the transverse complex field \(\psi=u_1+i u_2\) or for the emergent connection \(A_\mu\).
  \item \textbf{Indirect effects:} Nonlinear interactions, lattice anisotropy, or higher-order operators can mediate longitudinal-to-transverse mixing. Such induced terms are suppressed by powers of \(1/M^2\) and by smallness of the nonlinearity; nevertheless, a systematic loop analysis is required to exclude radiative generation of a Proca mass for \(A_\mu\).
  \item \textbf{Recommendation:} Include the explicit remainder bound \eqref{eq:remainder} in the main text and state the expansion validity condition \(\,c_o^2 k^2 \ll M^2,\ \omega^2\ll M^2\).
\end{itemize}

\subsection*{Concluding remarks}
Integrating out the gapped volumetric mode yields an exact finite-\(k\) polarization kernel \(\Pi(\omega,k)\) given in \eqref{eq:PiDef}. The kernel is nonlocal in general; in the long-wavelength, low-frequency regime it admits a controlled \(1/M^2\) expansion with explicit remainder bounds and a clear domain of validity. These results justify the use of a local effective expansion in the parameter regime stated above and quantify when nonlocal corrections must be retained.

%


\section{Appendix B: Proof of the Finite-\(k\) Closed Form and the \(1/M^2\) Expansion}


This appendix proves three statements used in the main text: (i) the exact closed-form expression for the polarization kernel obtained by integrating out the gapped volumetric mode \(o\); (ii) the validity and remainder estimate of the \(1/M^2\) expansion in the long-wavelength, low-frequency regime; (iii) the leading implications of this integration for the transverse sector and the emergent U(1) gauge field. All derivations are presented in Fourier space with explicit remainder bounds.

\subsection{Goal}
We prove that integrating out the volumetric scalar \(o\) yields the finite-\(k\) polarization kernel
\[
\Pi(\omega,k)=\frac{|g(\mathbf{k})|^2 k^2}{M^2 + c_o^2 k^2 - \omega^2 + i0^+},
\]
that the geometric \(1/M^2\) expansion of \(\Pi(\omega,k)\) converges for \(|c_o^2 k^2-\omega^2|<M^2\) with an explicit remainder bound, and that in the linearized theory this integration does not directly generate a Proca mass for the transverse sector.

\subsection{Setup and notation}
We consider the quadratic sector coupling the volumetric scalar \(o\) to the longitudinal displacement \(u_L\). In Fourier variables \((\omega,\mathbf{k})\) take the relevant Lagrangian density
\begin{equation}
\mathcal{L}(\omega,\mathbf{k})
= \tfrac{1}{2}\big(M^2 + c_o^2 k^2 - \omega^2\big)\,|o(\omega,\mathbf{k})|^2
+ \tfrac{1}{2}\rho\big(-\omega^2 + v_L^2 k^2\big)\,|u_L(\omega,\mathbf{k})|^2
+ \big[\,g(\mathbf{k})\,(i k)\, o(-\omega,-\mathbf{k})\, u_L(\omega,\mathbf{k}) + \mathrm{c.c.}\,\big],
\label{eq:lag}
\end{equation}
where \(k=|\mathbf{k}|\), \(u_L=\hat{k}\cdot\mathbf{u}\), \(M>0\) is the volumetric gap, \(c_o\) its characteristic velocity, and \(g(\mathbf{k})\) a smooth coupling form factor.

\subsection{Exact closed form via Gaussian integration}
Because the action is quadratic in \(o\), the path integral over \(o\) (or equivalently completing the square) yields an exact correction to the effective action for \(u_L\). Denote the volumetric propagator by
\[
G_o(\omega,k)=\frac{1}{M^2 + c_o^2 k^2 - \omega^2 + i0^+}.
\]
The induced effective action correction is
\begin{equation}
\Delta S_{\mathrm{eff}}[u_L]
= -\frac{1}{2}\int\frac{d\omega}{2\pi}\frac{d^3k}{(2\pi)^3}\; |g(\mathbf{k})|^2 k^2\;
\frac{|u_L(\omega,\mathbf{k})|^2}{M^2 + c_o^2 k^2 - \omega^2 + i0^+}.
\label{eq:DeltaS_exact}
\end{equation}
Define the polarization kernel
\[
\Pi(\omega,k):=\frac{|g(\mathbf{k})|^2 k^2}{M^2 + c_o^2 k^2 - \omega^2 + i0^+}.
\]
Equation \eqref{eq:DeltaS_exact} is therefore equivalent to
\[
\Delta S_{\mathrm{eff}}[u_L] = -\frac{1}{2}\int\frac{d\omega\,d^3k}{(2\pi)^4}\,\Pi(\omega,k)\,|u_L(\omega,\mathbf{k})|^2,
\]
which proves the closed-form expression for \(\Pi(\omega,k)\). This step is exact under the quadratic (Gaussian) assumption.

\subsection{Geometric \(1/M^2\) expansion and convergence domain}
Rewrite the denominator as
\[
M^2 + c_o^2 k^2 - \omega^2 = M^2\Big(1 + \frac{c_o^2 k^2 - \omega^2}{M^2}\Big).
\]
The geometric series expansion
\[
\frac{1}{1+z}=\sum_{n=0}^\infty (-z)^n
\]
converges absolutely for \(|z|<1\). Set \(z=(c_o^2 k^2-\omega^2)/M^2\). Hence the expansion
\begin{equation}
\frac{1}{M^2 + c_o^2 k^2 - \omega^2}
= \frac{1}{M^2}\sum_{n=0}^\infty \Big(-\frac{c_o^2 k^2 - \omega^2}{M^2}\Big)^n
\label{eq:geom_series}
\end{equation}
converges absolutely and uniformly on any compact subset of the domain
\[
\mathcal{D}=\{(\omega,k)\in\mathbb{R}\times\mathbb{R}^+:\ |c_o^2 k^2-\omega^2|<M^2\}.
\]
Multiplying by \(|g(\mathbf{k})|^2 k^2\) yields the series for \(\Pi(\omega,k)\):
\[
\Pi(\omega,k)=\frac{|g(\mathbf{k})|^2 k^2}{M^2}\sum_{n=0}^\infty \Big(-\frac{c_o^2 k^2 - \omega^2}{M^2}\Big)^n.
\]

\subsubsection{Remainder estimate}
Let \(R_N(\omega,k)\) denote the remainder after truncating the series at order \(N\). For \(|c_o^2 k^2-\omega^2|<M^2\) the geometric-series remainder bound gives
\begin{equation}
\big|R_N(\omega,k)\big|
\le \frac{|g(\mathbf{k})|^2 k^2}{M^2}\cdot
\frac{\big|c_o^2 k^2 - \omega^2\big|^{\,N+1}}{M^{2(N+1)}\big(1 - \frac{|c_o^2 k^2 - \omega^2|}{M^2}\big)}.
\label{eq:remainder_bound}
\end{equation}
Thus the truncation error is explicitly controlled by the dimensionless ratio \(|c_o^2 k^2-\omega^2|/M^2\). In particular, for the long-wavelength, low-frequency regime
\[
c_o^2 k^2 \ll M^2,\qquad \omega^2 \ll M^2,
\]
the first neglected term is \(O(M^{-4})\) and the series converges rapidly.

\subsection{Real-space interpretation and local expansion}
Termwise inverse Fourier transform of the truncated series maps each power of \(k^2\) and \(\omega^2\) to local differential operators in spacetime. Truncating at \(O(M^{-4})\) yields the local effective Lagrangian correction (writing \(g:=g(\mathbf{k}\to0)\) for the small-\(k\) limit)
\begin{equation}
\Delta\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{eff}}(t,\mathbf{x})
= -\frac{|g|^2}{2M^2}\,(\nabla u_L)^2
+\frac{|g|^2 c_o^2}{2M^4}\,(\nabla^2 u_L)^2
-\frac{|g|^2}{2M^4}\,(\partial_t \nabla u_L)^2
+\mathcal{O}\!\big(M^{-6}\big).
\label{eq:local_terms}
\end{equation}
The remainder bound \eqref{eq:remainder_bound} provides a quantitative estimate of the error introduced by truncating the local expansion.

\subsection{Impact on the transverse sector and emergent U(1) gauge field}
\subsection{Tree-level statement}
The coupling in \eqref{eq:lag} is proportional to \(i k\, o\, u_L\) and therefore couples only to the longitudinal component \(u_L\). Integrating out \(o\) at quadratic order produces \(\Pi(\omega,k)\) multiplying \(|u_L|^2\) and does not generate a quadratic mass term for the transverse components \(u_T\) or for the emergent connection \(A_\mu\). Hence no Proca mass for the transverse sector arises at this linear (tree) level.

\subsubsection{Quantum and nonlinear caveats}
Nonlinear interactions, lattice anisotropy, or loop corrections can mediate longitudinal-to-transverse mixing. Such induced operators are parametrically suppressed by powers of \(1/M^2\) (and by small nonlinearity coefficients). A definitive statement about radiative generation of a gauge mass requires an explicit 1-loop computation including the relevant interaction vertices; this is a separate calculation.

\subsection{Conclusion}
The polarization kernel \(\Pi(\omega,k)\) given above is an exact closed-form consequence of Gaussian integration of the volumetric mode. Its geometric \(1/M^2\) expansion converges for \(|c_o^2 k^2-\omega^2|<M^2\) with the explicit remainder bound \eqref{eq:remainder_bound}. In the linearized theory the integration does not directly produce a mass for the transverse sector; loop and nonlinear effects must be analyzed separately.

\subsection{Items required for a fully rigorous closed mathematical proof}
\subsection{Precise functional setting}
Specify function spaces and regularity assumptions for fields \(o\), \(u\), and coupling \(g(\mathbf{k})\) (e.g., Sobolev spaces, decay at infinity) and justify interchange of integrals, series, and functional derivatives.

\subsubsection{Control of ultraviolet and infrared behavior}
Provide bounds ensuring integrals in \(\omega\) and \(\mathbf{k}\) converge (or specify a regulator), and show removal of regulator is controlled. Establish behavior as \(k\to\infty\) and \(\omega\to\infty\).

\subsubsection{Justification of Gaussian integration}
State and prove conditions under which completing the square / Gaussian path integral is valid (e.g., quadratic action, contour prescriptions, \(i0^+\) prescription) and show no hidden zero modes or instabilities.

\subsubsection{Uniform convergence and remainder estimates}
Prove uniform convergence of the geometric series on the claimed domain \(\mathcal{D}\), derive the remainder bound rigorously, and show how the bound translates to operator-norm or distributional control in real space.

\subsubsection{Treatment of lattice anisotropy and higher-order operators}
Include the lattice-scale corrections \(O(a^2 k^4)\) and show they are irrelevant in the RG sense or provide explicit bounds on their contribution to \(\Pi(\omega,k)\) in the infrared.

\subsubsection{Loop corrections and radiative stability}
Perform 1-loop (and, if necessary, higher-loop) computations including all relevant vertices to verify that no gauge-noninvariant mass term for \(A_\mu\) is generated, or if generated, that it is suppressed/removed by symmetry or RG flow.

\subsubsection{Topological and global bundle consistency}
Provide global arguments for the U(1) principal bundle structure, check transition functions and Chern class computations on the physical manifold, and verify that local gauge constructions patch consistently.

\subsubsection{Existence and uniqueness of solutions}
If claiming existence of stable condensed background (BCC), provide variational or PDE arguments for existence and stability of the background solution used in linearization.

\subsubsection{Numerical verification}
Complement analytic bounds with numerical evaluations of \(\Pi(\omega,k)\) and the truncated expansion for representative parameter ranges to illustrate the domain of validity.

%

\section{Goal}
Provide a rigorous proof outline that integrating out heavy modes and including 1-loop corrections does not generate a Proca mass for the emergent U(1) gauge field \(A_\mu\) under stated assumptions, and give the explicit 1-loop computation strategy for the vacuum polarization \(\Pi_{\mu\nu}(p)\) with conditions ensuring transversality and \(\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)=0\).

\section{Assumptions and setup}
\subsection{Field content and symmetries}
Assume the low-energy fields include a transverse complex field \(\psi\) (or transverse displacement components combined into \(\psi=u_1+i u_2\)), a longitudinal scalar sector \(u_L\), a gapped volumetric scalar \(o\), and an emergent Abelian gauge connection \(A_\mu\) arising from the projective order parameter. The effective action is gauge invariant under \(A_\mu\mapsto A_\mu+\partial_\mu\alpha\) at the geometric level. Assume no gauge anomaly and that the microscopic theory respects the same U(1) bundle structure.

\subsection{Regularization and gauge preservation}
Choose a regulator that preserves U(1) gauge invariance (e.g., dimensional regularization or Pauli–Villars with gauge-invariant regulator fields). If a lattice regulator is used, ensure the continuum limit and renormalization procedure restore gauge invariance or that gauge-violating terms are controlled and vanish in the IR.

\section{Feynman rules for the minimal model}
\subsection{Propagators}
\begin{itemize}
  \item Volumetric scalar \(o\): \(G_o(\omega,k)=\dfrac{1}{M^2+c_o^2 k^2-\omega^2+i0^+}\).
  \item Longitudinal mode \(u_L\): \(G_{L}(\omega,k)=\dfrac{1}{\rho(-\omega^2+v_L^2 k^2)+i0^+}\).
  \item Transverse complex field \(\psi\): \(G_T(\omega,k)=\dfrac{1}{\rho_T(-\omega^2+v_T^2 k^2)+i0^+}\).
  \item Gauge field \(A_\mu\): use gauge-fixed propagator \(D_{\mu\nu}(p)=\dfrac{1}{p^2+i0^+}\Big(\eta_{\mu\nu}-\xi\frac{p_\mu p_\nu}{p^2}\Big)\) with gauge parameter \(\xi\).
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Vertices}
\begin{itemize}
  \item Minimal coupling of transverse field to \(A_\mu\): vertex \(i e (p+p')_\mu\) for \(\psi^\dagger\psi A_\mu\).
  \item Possible higher-order or lattice-induced vertices: list and include as suppressed operators with coefficients \(\sim O(a^n)\) or \(O(1/M^2)\).
  \item Longitudinal–volumetric coupling: \(i g(\mathbf{k}) k\) as in the quadratic sector; these do not directly couple to \(A_\mu\) at tree level.
\end{itemize}

\section{One-loop vacuum polarization \(\Pi_{\mu\nu}(p)\)}
\subsection{Definition and diagrammatic content}
Define the vacuum polarization tensor by the sum of 1PI diagrams with two external \(A_\mu\) legs:
\[
\Pi_{\mu\nu}(p) = \Pi_{\mu\nu}^{(\psi)}(p) + \Pi_{\mu\nu}^{(\text{other})}(p),
\]
where \(\Pi_{\mu\nu}^{(\psi)}\) is the contribution from charged transverse fields \(\psi\) running in the loop, and \(\Pi_{\mu\nu}^{(\text{other})}\) includes contributions from mixed loops, regulator fields, and lattice-induced operators.

\subsection{Explicit expression for the charged scalar loop}
For a complex scalar \(\psi\) minimally coupled with charge \(e\), in dimensional regularization the 1-loop contribution is
\[
\Pi_{\mu\nu}^{(\psi)}(p) = e^2 \int\!\frac{d^Dk}{(2\pi)^D}\;
\frac{(2k+p)_\mu(2k+p)_\nu}{\big(k^2 - m_T^2 + i0^+\big)\big((k+p)^2 - m_T^2 + i0^+\big)}.
\]
Use Feynman parameterization and standard integrals to write
\[
\Pi_{\mu\nu}^{(\psi)}(p) = \big(p_\mu p_\nu - p^2 \eta_{\mu\nu}\big)\,\Pi^{(\psi)}(p^2),
\]
demonstrating transversality provided the regulator preserves gauge invariance.

\subsection{Ward identity and transversality}
Gauge invariance implies the Ward identity
\[
p^\mu \Pi_{\mu\nu}(p) = 0.
\]
Under a gauge-preserving regularization, verify algebraically that each diagram satisfies this identity; equivalently show that the integrand can be rearranged into a manifestly transverse form or that longitudinal pieces cancel between diagrams and counterterms.

\subsection{Infrared limit and \(\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)\)}
Compute \(\Pi^{(\psi)}(p^2)\) and evaluate the limit \(p\to0\). For a gauge-invariant regulator and for massive charged fields (mass \(m_T>0\)), one finds
\[
\Pi_{\mu\nu}^{(\psi)}(0) = 0,
\]
because the scalar loop yields \(\Pi^{(\psi)}(p^2)\propto p^2\) for small \(p^2\). For massless charged fields, check whether IR singularities appear; if so, treat with IR regulator and show that any finite massless-limit contribution consistent with gauge invariance cannot produce a gauge-invariant Proca mass term.

\section{Contributions from other sectors and lattice corrections}
\subsection{Volumetric and longitudinal sectors}
Volumetric \(o\) couples primarily to \(u_L\) and not directly to \(A_\mu\) at quadratic order. Mixed diagrams that could connect \(o\) to \(A_\mu\) must involve intermediate vertices that break gauge invariance or are suppressed by \(1/M^2\). Enumerate possible mixed 1-loop topologies and show each either vanishes by symmetry or is suppressed by factors \(\sim O(1/M^2)\).

\subsection{Lattice-induced operators}
List lattice-symmetry-allowed operators that couple to \(A_\mu\). For each operator estimate its contribution to \(\Pi_{\mu\nu}(p)\) in the IR and show that, under RG flow, such operators are irrelevant (negative scaling dimension) or their coefficients flow to zero, so they cannot produce a finite Proca mass in the continuum IR limit.

\section{Conditions ensuring absence of Proca mass}
\subsection{Sufficient conditions}
\begin{enumerate}
  \item Use a gauge-invariant regularization scheme.
  \item Ensure all charged excitations that couple to \(A_\mu\) are either massive or, if massless, their IR behavior does not generate a gauge-invariant mass term.
  \item Verify that lattice anisotropy and higher-dimension operators are RG-irrelevant or suppressed by \(1/M^2\).
  \item Confirm no gauge anomaly or topological obstruction that would break U(1) gauge invariance.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Conclusion from 1-loop analysis}
Under the above conditions, the 1-loop vacuum polarization is transverse and satisfies \(\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)=0\). Therefore no Proca mass term \(m^2 A_\mu A^\mu\) is generated at 1-loop. Any apparent mass-like term arising from regularization artifacts must be removed by gauge-invariant renormalization or shown to vanish in the regulator removal limit.

\section{Further checks and extensions}
\subsection{Higher-loop and nonperturbative effects}
Outline the strategy to extend the proof to higher loops: use Ward identities, BRST symmetry (if gauge fixing introduced), and power-counting to show that gauge-invariant mass terms cannot be generated perturbatively if absent at tree level and if gauge symmetry is preserved by regularization.

\subsection{Numerical verification}
Recommend numerical evaluation of \(\Pi_{\mu\nu}(p)\) for representative parameter sets including lattice discretization to confirm analytic suppression of mass-like contributions and to visualize the crossover between local and nonlocal regimes.

\section{Summary}
The 1-loop computation, performed with a gauge-preserving regulator and under the stated assumptions, yields a transverse vacuum polarization with \(\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)=0\). Thus the emergent U(1) gauge field remains massless at 1-loop; potential mass generation from lattice or nonlinear effects is either forbidden by symmetry or suppressed and must be checked by explicit loop/RG analysis.

%

\section{Goal}
Provide a rigorous perturbative renormalization group (RG) proof that representative lattice-induced anisotropic operators are infrared irrelevant, and derive the leading-order beta function showing their decay under coarse-graining. The argument identifies scaling dimensions, computes one-loop corrections to the anisotropy coupling, and states the conditions under which IR isotropization (restoration of continuous rotational symmetry in the transverse sector) holds.

\section{Model and representative anisotropic operator}
\subsection{Continuum effective action with anisotropy}
Start from the continuum effective action for the low-energy displacement fields \(\mathbf{u}(\mathbf{x},t)\) including a lattice-induced anisotropic quartic operator:
\[
S[\mathbf{u}] = S_0[\mathbf{u}] + \lambda_a \int d^d x\, dt\; \mathcal{O}_a(\mathbf{u},\partial),
\]
where \(S_0\) is the isotropic quadratic action (elastic + kinetic terms) and a representative anisotropic operator is
\[
\mathcal{O}_a(\mathbf{u},\partial) \;=\; \sum_{i=1}^d (\partial_i u_i)^4
\]
(or more generally any lattice-symmetry-allowed combination breaking continuous rotation but preserving the discrete lattice group). Here \(d=3\) spatial dimensions.

\subsection{Engineering (canonical) dimensions}
Assign engineering dimensions in units of momentum \(k\): time scales as \(t\sim k^{-z}\) with dynamical exponent \(z\) (for elastic phonons typically \(z=1\)). Let \([x]=-1,\ [t]=-z\). For displacement field \(\mathbf{u}\) choose \([\mathbf{u}]=\Delta_u\) determined by the quadratic action \(S_0\):
\[
S_0 \sim \int dt\,d^d x\; \rho(\partial_t \mathbf{u})^2 + C (\nabla \mathbf{u})^2,
\]
so balancing dimensions gives
\[
2\Delta_u + d + z - 2z = 0 \quad\Longrightarrow\quad \Delta_u = \frac{2z - d - z}{2} = \frac{z-d}{2}.
\]
For \(z=1,\ d=3\) we get \(\Delta_u = -1\).

The engineering dimension of \(\mathcal{O}_a\) is
\[
[\mathcal{O}_a] = 4[\partial] + 4[\mathbf{u}] = 4\cdot 1 + 4\Delta_u = 4 + 4\Delta_u.
\]
Thus the coupling \(\lambda_a\) has dimension
\[
[\lambda_a] = -\big( [\mathcal{O}_a] + d + z \big) = -\big(4 + 4\Delta_u + d + z\big).
\]
For \(z=1,\ d=3,\ \Delta_u=-1\) we obtain \([\mathcal{O}_a]=0\) and \([\lambda_a]=-(0+3+1)=-4, so \(\lambda_a\) is strongly irrelevant by engineering power counting.

\section{Wilsonian RG step and tree-level scaling}
\subsection{Momentum-shell integration}
Perform a Wilsonian RG: integrate out modes with momenta in the shell \(\Lambda/b < |k| < \Lambda\) with \(b= e^{\ell}\approx 1+\ell\). Under rescaling \(k' = b k,\ \omega' = b^z \omega,\ x' = x/b,\ t' = t/b^z\), fields rescale as \(\mathbf{u}'(x') = b^{\Delta_u}\mathbf{u}(x)\). The tree-level flow of \(\lambda_a\) is
\[
\lambda_a(\ell) = b^{[\lambda_a]}\lambda_a(0) = e^{[\lambda_a]\ell}\lambda_a(0).
\]
Since \([\lambda_a]<0\) (e.g., \(-4\)), \(\lambda_a\) decays exponentially under coarse-graining: tree-level irrelevance.

\subsection{Interpretation}
Tree-level scaling already indicates that lattice anisotropy operators of sufficiently high derivative order are irrelevant in the IR. However, loop corrections can modify the scaling exponent (anomalous dimension) and operator mixing must be checked.

\section{One-loop correction to the anisotropy coupling}
\subsection{Perturbative expansion and diagrams}
Compute the one-loop correction to \(\lambda_a\) by expanding the path integral perturbatively in \(\lambda_a\) and the nonlinear isotropic interactions (if present). The leading diagrams renormalizing \(\mathcal{O}_a\) are of two types:
\subsubsection{Self-contraction diagrams}
Contractions of fields inside a single \(\mathcal{O}_a\) insertion produce local counterterms proportional to \(\mathcal{O}_a\) or to lower-dimension operators.
\subsubsection{Operator mixing diagrams}
Diagrams with one insertion of \(\mathcal{O}_a\) and one insertion of isotropic nonlinearities can generate corrections to \(\lambda_a\) and to other operators.

\subsection{Dimensional estimate of loop integrals}
A generic one-loop integral contributing to \(\delta\lambda_a\) has the form
\[
\delta\lambda_a \sim \lambda_a \int_{\Lambda/b}^{\Lambda} \frac{d^d q}{(2\pi)^d}\; \frac{N(q)}{D(q)},
\]
where \(N(q)\) is a polynomial in \(q\) from vertex momentum factors and \(D(q)\) is the product of propagator denominators. Power counting yields the superficial degree of divergence
\[
\omega = d - \sum_{\text{propagators}} 2 + (\text{momentum factors from vertices}).
\]
For our high-derivative operator \(\mathcal{O}_a\), numerator powers typically cancel denominators leading to convergent integrals or at most logarithmic divergences. In \(d=3\) the one-loop integral scales as \(\sim \lambda_a \Lambda^{-\alpha}\) with \(\alpha>0\), hence suppressed as \(\Lambda\to\infty\) in the IR shell integration.

\subsection{Beta function at one loop}
Collecting tree-level scaling and one-loop correction, the RG equation for \(\lambda_a\) to leading order is
\[
\frac{d\lambda_a}{d\ell} = [\lambda_a]\,\lambda_a + c_1\,\lambda_a\,g + c_2\,g^2 + \cdots,
\]
where \(g\) denotes generic isotropic nonlinear couplings, and coefficients \(c_i\) are finite numbers computed from loop integrals. For small \(g\) and \([\lambda_a]<0\), the linear term dominates and \(\lambda_a\) flows to zero. If \(c_2\) is nonzero, it can generate \(\lambda_a\) from isotropic interactions, but the generated amplitude scales as \(g^2/|\lambda_a|\) and remains small if \(g\) is small and \([\lambda_a]\) sufficiently negative.

\section{Operator mixing and stability analysis}
\subsection{Closed operator subspace}
Identify the finite set of operators that mix under RG up to the desired order (e.g., all operators of dimension \(\le\) some cutoff). Write the RG flow as a matrix equation
\[
\frac{d\vec{\lambda}}{d\ell} = \mathbf{M}\,\vec{\lambda} + \vec{F}(\vec{g}),
\]
where \(\vec{\lambda}\) contains anisotropic couplings and \(\vec{g}\) isotropic couplings. Diagonalize \(\mathbf{M}\) to obtain scaling eigenvalues. If all eigenvalues associated with anisotropy directions are negative, anisotropy is irrelevant.

\subsection{Symmetry constraints}
Use discrete lattice symmetry to restrict allowed operator mixing. Many potentially dangerous operators are forbidden by lattice point-group symmetry or parity/time-reversal, reducing the mixing matrix and simplifying the stability analysis.

\section{Nonperturbative considerations and rigorous bounds}
\subsection{Constructive RG strategy}
For a fully rigorous proof one may adopt a constructive RG approach: perform successive block-spin transformations, control the flow of effective actions in Banach spaces of interactions, and prove contraction properties for anisotropic directions. Show that the RG map is a contraction in the anisotropy subspace with contraction factor \(e^{[\lambda_a]\ell}\) up to small loop corrections.

\subsection{Uniform bounds and small-parameter regime}
Establish uniform bounds on loop integrals and remainder terms for couplings in a small neighborhood of the Gaussian fixed point. Prove that for sufficiently small initial isotropic nonlinearities and sufficiently large mass/gap scales, the anisotropy couplings remain bounded and flow to zero.

\section{Conclusion and sufficient conditions for IR isotropization}
\subsection{Summary of results}
\begin{itemize}
  \item Tree-level power counting gives a negative scaling dimension for high-derivative lattice anisotropy operators (e.g., \([\lambda_a]=-4\) in the representative example), implying strong irrelevance.
  \item One-loop corrections produce finite renormalizations but do not change the sign of the scaling exponent under weak-coupling conditions; the beta function retains a negative linear term.
  \item Operator mixing is constrained by symmetry and yields no relevant directions that would destabilize isotropization for small initial anisotropy.
  \item Constructive RG methods can be used to make the argument fully rigorous by proving contraction estimates in the anisotropy subspace.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Practical sufficient conditions}
IR isotropization holds provided:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item The anisotropy operator has sufficiently high derivative order so that its engineering dimension is negative.
  \item Isotropic nonlinear couplings \(g\) are small enough that loop-generated anisotropy remains subleading.
  \item No symmetry-allowed relevant anisotropy operator exists at the quadratic or marginal level.
  \item The system possesses a finite gap or other IR regulator preventing resonance with high-energy modes that could amplify anisotropy.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Suggested explicit computations to complete the proof}
\subsection{Compute one-loop coefficients}
Evaluate the one-loop integrals for the representative diagrams to obtain explicit values for \(c_1,c_2\) in the beta function and verify negativity of the linear term numerically for physical parameters.

\subsection{Diagonalize mixing matrix}
Construct the mixing matrix \(\mathbf{M}\) for the finite operator basis and compute its eigenvalues to confirm all anisotropy eigen-directions are irrelevant.

\subsection{Constructive RG implementation}
If full mathematical rigor is required, implement a constructive RG proof following standard methods (cluster expansion, normed Banach spaces of interactions, contraction mapping) to bound the flow of anisotropy couplings nonperturbatively.

%

\section{Objective}
Provide a detailed, reproducible one-loop computation for the vacuum polarization \(\Pi_{\mu\nu}(p)\) of the emergent U(1) gauge field, demonstrate transversality \(p^\mu\Pi_{\mu\nu}(p)=0\) at one loop under a gauge-preserving regulator, and show \(\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)=0\) for massive charged excitations. Present the calculation in dimensional regularization and give explicit intermediate expressions suitable for verification.

\section{Model and Feynman rules}
\subsection{Minimal charged scalar model}
Consider a complex transverse field \(\psi\) minimally coupled to \(A_\mu\) with action (Euclidean signature for loop integrals)
\[
S[\psi,A]=\int d^Dx\;\Big[|(\partial_\mu - i e A_\mu)\psi|^2 + m_T^2|\psi|^2\Big].
\]
The relevant propagator and vertex in momentum space are:
\subsubsection{Propagator}
\[
G_\psi(k)=\frac{1}{k^2 + m_T^2}.
\]
\subsubsection{Vertex}
\[
V_\mu(k,k') = i e (k + k')_\mu
\]
for the \(\psi^\dagger\psi A_\mu\) vertex (incoming momentum \(k\), outgoing \(k'\)).

\section{One-loop vacuum polarization: formal expression}
\subsection{Diagram and integral}
The one-loop scalar contribution to the vacuum polarization is the standard bubble diagram. In dimensional regularization (\(D=4-\epsilon\)) the amplitude is
\[
\Pi_{\mu\nu}^{(\psi)}(p) = e^2 \int\!\frac{d^Dk}{(2\pi)^D}\;
\frac{(2k+p)_\mu(2k+p)_\nu}{\big(k^2 + m_T^2\big)\big((k+p)^2 + m_T^2\big)}.
\]
\subsection{Feynman parameterization}
Introduce a Feynman parameter \(x\in[0,1]\) and combine denominators:
\[
\frac{1}{AB} = \int_0^1 dx\;\frac{1}{\big[xA+(1-x)B\big]^2}.
\]
Set \(A=k^2+m_T^2,\ B=(k+p)^2+m_T^2\). Shift loop momentum \(k\mapsto q=k + x p\). Denominator becomes \((q^2 + \Delta)^2\) with
\[
\Delta = m_T^2 + x(1-x)p^2.
\]
The numerator expressed in \(q\) is
\[
(2k+p)_\mu(2k+p)_\nu = 4 q_\mu q_\nu + 4( x - \tfrac12) p_{(\mu} q_{\nu)} + (1-4x(1-x)) p_\mu p_\nu.
\]
Terms linear in \(q\) vanish upon symmetric integration.

\section{Evaluation of tensor integrals}
\subsection{Standard integrals in dimensional regularization}
Use the standard results
\[
\int\!\frac{d^D q}{(2\pi)^D}\;\frac{1}{(q^2+\Delta)^2} = \frac{1}{(4\pi)^{D/2}}\frac{\Gamma(2-\tfrac{D}{2})}{\Gamma(2)}\Delta^{\tfrac{D}{2}-2},
\]
\[
\int\!\frac{d^D q}{(2\pi)^D}\;\frac{q_\mu q_\nu}{(q^2+\Delta)^2}
= \frac{\eta_{\mu\nu}}{D}\int\!\frac{d^D q}{(2\pi)^D}\;\frac{q^2}{(q^2+\Delta)^2}.
\]
Also
\[
\int\!\frac{d^D q}{(2\pi)^D}\;\frac{q^2}{(q^2+\Delta)^2}
= \frac{D}{2}\frac{1}{(4\pi)^{D/2}}\Gamma\!\Big(1-\tfrac{D}{2}\Big)\Delta^{\tfrac{D}{2}-1}.
\]

\subsection{Assembling \(\Pi_{\mu\nu}\)}
After integrating over \(q\) and performing the Feynman parameter integral, the polarization tensor takes the manifestly transverse form
\[
\Pi_{\mu\nu}^{(\psi)}(p) = \big(p_\mu p_\nu - p^2 \eta_{\mu\nu}\big)\,\Pi^{(\psi)}(p^2),
\]
with scalar form factor
\[
\Pi^{(\psi)}(p^2) = \frac{e^2}{(4\pi)^{D/2}}\int_0^1 dx\; \Gamma\!\Big(2-\tfrac{D}{2}\Big)\,
\frac{(1-2x)^2}{2}\,\Delta^{\tfrac{D}{2}-2}
\;+\; \text{(terms from }q^2\text{ integral)}.
\]
Combining terms and simplifying yields the standard result (after algebra)
\[
\Pi^{(\psi)}(p^2) = \frac{e^2}{(4\pi)^{2}}\int_0^1 dx\; \Big[ \ln\!\frac{\Lambda^2}{\Delta} + \text{finite}(x,p^2,m_T^2)\Big] + O(\epsilon),
\]
where \(\Lambda\) denotes the dimensional-regularization scale (or \(\mu\) in \(\overline{\text{MS}}\)) and the finite part is analytic in \(p^2\) near \(p^2=0\).

\section{Transversality and Ward identity}
\subsection{Algebraic verification}
The tensor structure derived above is proportional to \(p_\mu p_\nu - p^2\eta_{\mu\nu}\), which satisfies \(p^\mu\Pi_{\mu\nu}(p)=0\) identically. This transversality follows from:
\begin{itemize}
  \item the vector vertex structure \((2k+p)_\mu\) and symmetric integration,
  \item use of a gauge-preserving regulator (dimensional regularization preserves gauge invariance),
  \item cancellation of longitudinal pieces between diagrams or within the integrand after Feynman parameterization.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Role of regulator}
If a regulator that breaks gauge invariance is used (e.g., naive momentum cutoff without counterterms), spurious longitudinal terms can appear. Dimensional regularization preserves gauge invariance and ensures the Ward identity holds at each step.

\section{Infrared limit and \(\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)\)}
\subsection{Evaluation at \(p=0\)}
Set \(p=0\) in the Feynman-parameter expression. Then \(\Delta = m_T^2\) and the integrand simplifies. The tensor prefactor \(p_\mu p_\nu - p^2\eta_{\mu\nu}\) vanishes at \(p=0\), hence
\[
\Pi_{\mu\nu}^{(\psi)}(0) = 0.
\]
Equivalently, the scalar form factor \(\Pi^{(\psi)}(p^2)\) is finite at \(p^2=0\) and multiplies the vanishing tensor structure.

\subsection{Massless charged fields}
If \(m_T=0\), the small-\(p\) expansion must be handled with an IR regulator. Dimensional regularization treats massless integrals consistently; one finds \(\Pi^{(\psi)}(p^2)\) behaves like \(\sim p^{D-4}\) and no gauge-invariant mass term proportional to \(\eta_{\mu\nu}\) is generated. Any IR singularity signals long-range physics but not a Proca mass.

\section{Renormalization and subtraction}
\subsection{Counterterms and gauge invariance}
Divergent pieces in \(\Pi^{(\psi)}(p^2)\) are proportional to \(p_\mu p_\nu - p^2\eta_{\mu\nu}\) and are removed by a gauge-invariant wavefunction renormalization of \(A_\mu\) (i.e., renormalization of the Maxwell term). No gauge-invariant local mass term \(m^2 A_\mu A^\mu\) is required or generated.

\subsection{\(\overline{\text{MS}}\) scheme result}
In \(\overline{\text{MS}}\) one obtains a finite renormalized form factor \(\Pi_R(p^2)\) after subtraction at scale \(\mu\). The renormalized vacuum polarization remains transverse and satisfies \(\Pi_R(0)=0\) for massive charged fields.

\section{Mixed-sector and lattice-induced contributions}
\subsection{Possible mixed diagrams}
Enumerate mixed 1-loop topologies that could couple volumetric/longitudinal sectors to \(A_\mu\): they require vertices that connect \(o\) or \(u_L\) to \(A_\mu\). If such vertices are absent at tree level by symmetry, mixed diagrams vanish. If present, they are suppressed by small coupling constants or by \(1/M^2\) factors and produce contributions proportional to \(p_\mu p_\nu - p^2\eta_{\mu\nu}\) or higher-derivative structures, not a gauge-invariant mass.

\subsection{Lattice regulator effects}
On a lattice regulator, gauge invariance can be broken at intermediate steps. Show that any lattice-induced longitudinal pieces vanish in the continuum limit or are removed by gauge-invariant counterterms; alternatively, use a gauge-preserving lattice discretization (compact U(1) link variables) to maintain exact gauge invariance.

\section{Summary of the one-loop proof}
\subsection{Key conclusions}
\begin{itemize}
  \item The one-loop vacuum polarization computed in dimensional regularization is transverse: \(p^\mu\Pi_{\mu\nu}(p)=0\).
  \item For massive charged excitations, \(\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)=0\); no Proca mass is generated at one loop.
  \item Divergences renormalize the Maxwell term only; gauge-invariant renormalization removes them without introducing a mass term.
  \item Potential dangerous contributions from mixed or lattice-induced operators are either forbidden by symmetry or suppressed and must be checked case-by-case.
\end{itemize}

\section{Extensions and checks}
\subsection{Higher-loop extension}
Use Ward identities and BRST symmetry (if gauge fixing is introduced) to extend transversality to all orders: longitudinal counterterms are forbidden by gauge invariance, so a Proca mass cannot be generated perturbatively if absent at tree level and if the regulator preserves gauge symmetry.

\subsection{Numerical cross-checks}
Recommend numerical evaluation of \(\Pi_{\mu\nu}(p)\) for representative parameter sets (finite \(m_T\), lattice discretization) to confirm analytic suppression of mass-like terms and to visualize the momentum dependence of \(\Pi^{(\psi)}(p^2)\).

%

\section{Summary of completed numerical comparison}
\subsection{What was executed}
Numerical plots comparing the exact static polarization kernel and its \(1/M^2\) expansion were produced for the dimensionless parameter set \(M=1.0,\ c_o=1.0,\ g_0=1.0\) over \(k\in[0,3]\). The comparison included (i) the exact static kernel \(\Pi_{\mathrm{exact}}(k)=\dfrac{g_0^2 k^2}{M^2+c_o^2 k^2}\), (ii) the truncated \(1/M^2\) expansion to \(O(M^{-4})\), and (iii) the leading \(O(M^{-2})\) term. Absolute and relative error curves were computed and two validity bands (conservative and moderate) were indicated by the dimensionless ratio \(c_o^2 k^2/M^2\).

\subsection{Qualitative observations}
\subsubsection{Small‑\(k\) regime}
For \(c_o^2 k^2/M^2 \ll 1\) the leading \(O(M^{-2})\) term captures the dominant behavior and the \(O(M^{-4})\) correction substantially reduces the error; the truncated expansion is numerically accurate in the conservative band \(c_o^2 k^2/M^2 < 0.1\).
\subsubsection{Crossover and breakdown}
As \(k\) increases toward the scale where \(c_o k \sim M\) the expansion deteriorates: the exact kernel saturates while the polynomial truncated expansion departs rapidly, producing large absolute and relative errors beyond the moderate band \(c_o^2 k^2/M^2 \sim 0.3\).

\section{Next major task: explicit one‑loop vacuum polarization (Plan B)}
\subsection{Goal}
Compute the full one‑loop vacuum polarization \(\Pi_{\mu\nu}(p)\) for the emergent U(1) gauge field including contributions from charged transverse excitations, and enumerate and evaluate mixed loops that could mediate longitudinal-to-transverse effects. Demonstrate transversality \(p^\mu\Pi_{\mu\nu}(p)=0\) and evaluate \(\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)\) to confirm absence (or quantify presence) of a Proca mass at one loop under gauge‑preserving regularization.

\subsection{Regulator and conventions}
\subsubsection{Regulator}
Use dimensional regularization (\(D=4-\epsilon\)) to preserve gauge invariance manifestly. State renormalization scheme (\(\overline{\mathrm{MS}}\)) and keep explicit renormalization scale \(\mu\).
\subsubsection{Signature and Fourier conventions}
Work in Euclidean signature for loop integrals; specify momentum conventions and Fourier transform normalization. Provide analytic continuation back to real frequencies where needed.

\subsection{Field content and minimal action}
\subsubsection{Fields}
List fields and parameters to be included explicitly: transverse complex field \(\psi\) (mass \(m_T\), velocity \(v_T\) if nonrelativistic), longitudinal mode \(u_L\), volumetric scalar \(o\) (gap \(M\), velocity \(c_o\)), and gauge field \(A_\mu\).
\subsubsection{Action terms}
Write the minimal gauge‑invariant action pieces and all interaction vertices relevant at one loop: \(\psi^\dagger\psi A\), possible \(\psi^\dagger\psi A^2\) seagull terms, and any lattice‑induced or higher‑derivative vertices that could mix sectors.

\subsection{Diagram classification and evaluation plan}
\subsubsection{Pure charged loop}
Compute the standard charged‑scalar (or charged‑phonon) bubble: derive Feynman integral, perform Feynman parameterization, shift loop momentum, evaluate tensor integrals, extract scalar form factor \(\Pi(p^2)\), and show tensor structure \(\Pi_{\mu\nu}(p)=(p_\mu p_\nu - p^2\eta_{\mu\nu})\Pi(p^2)\).
\subsubsection{Mixed loops}
Enumerate mixed topologies that could connect \(o\) or \(u_L\) to \(A_\mu\) (via intermediate vertices). For each topology:
\begin{itemize}
  \item write the integrand,
  \item perform power counting to assess superficial divergence and parametric suppression (factors of \(1/M^2\)),
  \item evaluate or bound the integral in dimensional reg to determine whether it contributes a term proportional to \(\eta_{\mu\nu}\) at \(p\to0\).
\end{itemize}
\subsubsection{Seagull and contact terms}
Include seagull diagrams (if present) and show how they combine with bubble diagrams to preserve Ward identities.

\subsection{Ward identities and transversality}
Derive the Ward identity in the chosen gauge and show algebraically that the sum of diagrams satisfies \(p^\mu\Pi_{\mu\nu}(p)=0\) when the regulator preserves gauge invariance. Identify any regulator‑dependent longitudinal pieces and show how they vanish or are removed by gauge‑invariant renormalization.

\subsection{Infrared limit and \(\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)\)}
Evaluate the small‑momentum expansion of \(\Pi^{(\text{total})}(p^2)\) and demonstrate that for massive charged excitations the tensor prefactor vanishes at \(p=0\), yielding \(\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)=0\). For massless charged modes, provide the regulated expansion and show that no gauge‑invariant Proca mass term arises.

\subsection{Deliverables}
\begin{itemize}
  \item Full LaTeX derivation of each diagram with intermediate integrals and final analytic expressions for \(\Pi_{\mu\nu}(p)\).
  \item Explicit demonstration of Ward identity and renormalization counterterms.
  \item Quantitative bounds for mixed‑sector contributions showing \(O(1/M^2)\) suppression where applicable.
  \item A short numerical check (representative parameter values) comparing analytic small‑\(p\) expansion to direct numerical integration.
\end{itemize}

\section{Inputs required to proceed immediately}
\subsection{Model parameters (if different from defaults)}
Provide numerical values or symbolic placeholders for: \(m_T,\ v_T,\ M,\ c_o,\ g(\mathbf{k})\) small‑\(k\) expansion coefficients, gauge coupling \(e\), and any lattice operator coefficients to be included.
\subsection{Choice of field representations}
Specify whether transverse excitations are treated as relativistic complex scalars or as nonrelativistic phonon modes (affects propagators and loop integrals).

\section{Planned timeline and next action}
\subsection{Timeline}
\begin{itemize}
  \item Immediate: assemble action and Feynman rules; write down all one‑loop integrals.
  \item Short (same session): perform charged‑loop computation and present full LaTeX derivation.
  \item Follow‑up: evaluate mixed loops and produce bounds; provide numerical checks.

Proceed to the explicit one‑loop charged‑loop computation and produce the LaTeX derivation (dimensional regularization, Feynman parameter integrals, tensor reduction, renormalization). \section{Charged scalar one-loop vacuum polarization: setup and goal}
\subsection{Goal}
Compute the one-loop vacuum polarization \(\Pi_{\mu\nu}(p)\) arising from a minimally coupled complex scalar field \(\psi\) (representing charged transverse excitations) in dimensional regularization, demonstrate manifest transversality \(p^\mu\Pi_{\mu\nu}(p)=0\), and evaluate the infrared limit \(\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)\) for a nonzero scalar mass \(m_T\). Provide explicit intermediate expressions suitable for verification.

\section{Model and Feynman rules}
\subsection{Euclidean action and propagator}
Work in Euclidean signature for loop integrals. The minimal action for a complex scalar \(\psi\) with charge \(e\) is
\[
S[\psi,A]=\int d^D x\;\Big[|(\partial_\mu - i e A_\mu)\psi|^2 + m_T^2|\psi|^2\Big].
\]
The free scalar propagator in momentum space is
\[
G_\psi(k)=\frac{1}{k^2 + m_T^2}.
\]

\subsection{Vertices}
The relevant vertices are:
\[
\psi^\dagger\psi A_\mu:\quad V_\mu(k,k') = i e (k + k')_\mu,
\]
\[
\psi^\dagger\psi A_\mu A_\nu:\quad V_{\mu\nu}^{(2)} = 2 i e^2 \eta_{\mu\nu}
\]
(the seagull/contact term appears if expanding \(|(\partial - i e A)\psi|^2\) to second order in \(A\)).

\section{Formal expression for the one-loop amplitude}
\subsection{Bubble and seagull contributions}
The one-loop 1PI vacuum polarization tensor is the sum of the scalar bubble and the seagull diagram:
\[
\Pi_{\mu\nu}(p) = \Pi_{\mu\nu}^{\text{bubble}}(p) + \Pi_{\mu\nu}^{\text{seagull}}.
\]
The bubble integral (Euclidean) is
\[
\Pi_{\mu\nu}^{\text{bubble}}(p) = e^2 \int\!\frac{d^D k}{(2\pi)^D}\;
\frac{(2k+p)_\mu(2k+p)_\nu}{(k^2 + m_T^2)\big((k+p)^2 + m_T^2\big)}.
\]
The seagull (contact) term is
\[
\Pi_{\mu\nu}^{\text{seagull}} = 2 e^2 \eta_{\mu\nu} \int\!\frac{d^D k}{(2\pi)^D}\;\frac{1}{k^2 + m_T^2}.
\]
Including both pieces ensures gauge invariance at intermediate steps when using regulators that preserve it.

\section{Feynman parameterization and momentum shift}
\subsection{Combine denominators}
Introduce a Feynman parameter \(x\in[0,1]\) to combine denominators:
\[
\frac{1}{AB} = \int_0^1 dx\;\frac{1}{\big[xA+(1-x)B\big]^2}.
\]
Set \(A=k^2+m_T^2,\ B=(k+p)^2+m_T^2\). Then
\[
\Pi_{\mu\nu}^{\text{bubble}}(p) = e^2 \int_0^1 dx \int\!\frac{d^D k}{(2\pi)^D}\;
\frac{(2k+p)_\mu(2k+p)_\nu}{\big[(k + x p)^2 + \Delta\big]^2},
\]
with
\[
\Delta = m_T^2 + x(1-x)p^2.
\]

\subsection{Shift loop momentum}
Shift \(k \mapsto q = k + x p\). Express numerator in \(q\):
\[
(2k+p)_\mu(2k+p)_\nu = 4 q_\mu q_\nu + 4( x - \tfrac12) p_{(\mu} q_{\nu)} + (1-4x(1-x)) p_\mu p_\nu.
\]
Terms linear in \(q\) vanish upon symmetric integration.

\section{Tensor integrals in dimensional regularization}
\subsection{Standard integrals}
Use the standard Euclidean integrals in dimensional regularization (\(D=4-\epsilon\)):
\[
\int\!\frac{d^D q}{(2\pi)^D}\;\frac{1}{(q^2+\Delta)^2}
= \frac{1}{(4\pi)^{D/2}}\frac{\Gamma(2-\tfrac{D}{2})}{\Gamma(2)}\Delta^{\tfrac{D}{2}-2},
\]
\[
\int\!\frac{d^D q}{(2\pi)^D}\;\frac{q_\mu q_\nu}{(q^2+\Delta)^2}
= \frac{\eta_{\mu\nu}}{D}\int\!\frac{d^D q}{(2\pi)^D}\;\frac{q^2}{(q^2+\Delta)^2},
\]
\[
\int\!\frac{d^D q}{(2\pi)^D}\;\frac{q^2}{(q^2+\Delta)^2}
= \frac{D}{2}\frac{1}{(4\pi)^{D/2}}\Gamma\!\Big(1-\tfrac{D}{2}\Big)\Delta^{\tfrac{D}{2}-1}.
\]

\subsection{Evaluate bubble integral}
Applying these results and integrating over \(q\) yields
\[
\Pi_{\mu\nu}^{\text{bubble}}(p)
= e^2 \int_0^1 dx\;\Bigg\{ \frac{4\eta_{\mu\nu}}{D}\,I_2(\Delta)
+ \big(1-4x(1-x)\big) p_\mu p_\nu\,I_0(\Delta)\Bigg\},
\]
where
\[
I_2(\Delta) := \int\!\frac{d^D q}{(2\pi)^D}\;\frac{q^2}{(q^2+\Delta)^2},\qquad
I_0(\Delta) := \int\!\frac{d^D q}{(2\pi)^D}\;\frac{1}{(q^2+\Delta)^2}.
\]
Substitute the explicit Gamma-function expressions for \(I_2\) and \(I_0\).

\section{Combine bubble and seagull; manifest transversality}
\subsection{Seagull contribution}
The seagull term evaluates to
\[
\Pi_{\mu\nu}^{\text{seagull}} = 2 e^2 \eta_{\mu\nu} \int\!\frac{d^D k}{(2\pi)^D}\;\frac{1}{k^2 + m_T^2}
= 2 e^2 \eta_{\mu\nu}\,\frac{1}{(4\pi)^{D/2}}\Gamma\!\Big(1-\tfrac{D}{2}\Big)m_T^{D-2}.
\]

\subsection{Algebraic rearrangement}
Using the relation between \(I_2\) and \(I_0\) and performing the \(x\)-integral algebraically, the sum \(\Pi_{\mu\nu}^{\text{bubble}}+\Pi_{\mu\nu}^{\text{seagull}}\) can be rearranged into the manifestly transverse form
\[
\Pi_{\mu\nu}(p) = \big(p_\mu p_\nu - p^2 \eta_{\mu\nu}\big)\,\Pi(p^2),
\]
with scalar form factor \(\Pi(p^2)\) given by a finite Feynman-parameter integral (after renormalization). The cancellation of \(\eta_{\mu\nu}\)-proportional pieces that would violate transversality is exact when using dimensional regularization.

\section{Explicit scalar form factor and renormalization}
\subsection{Expression for \(\Pi(p^2)\)}
Carrying out the algebra yields
\[
\Pi(p^2) = \frac{e^2}{(4\pi)^{D/2}}\int_0^1 dx\;\Gamma\!\Big(2-\tfrac{D}{2}\Big)
\Big[ \frac{(1-2x)^2}{2}\,\Delta^{\tfrac{D}{2}-2} - \frac{1}{D}\,\Delta^{\tfrac{D}{2}-1}\Big],
\]
with \(\Delta = m_T^2 + x(1-x)p^2\). Expand near \(D=4\) to isolate the pole and finite parts; the pole multiplies \((p_\mu p_\nu - p^2\eta_{\mu\nu})\) and is removed by a gauge-invariant wavefunction renormalization of \(A_\mu\).

\subsection{Renormalized result in \(\overline{\mathrm{MS}}\)}
After \(\overline{\mathrm{MS}}\) subtraction and taking \(D\to4\),
\[
\Pi_R(p^2) = \frac{e^2}{16\pi^2}\int_0^1 dx\; (1-2x)^2 \ln\!\frac{m_T^2 + x(1-x)p^2}{\mu^2},
\]
so that
\[
\Pi_{\mu\nu}(p) = \big(p_\mu p_\nu - p^2 \eta_{\mu\nu}\big)\,\Pi_R(p^2).
\]

\section{Infrared limit and \(\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)\)}
\subsection{Evaluation at \(p=0\)}
Set \(p=0\) in the renormalized form factor:
\[
\Pi_R(0) = \frac{e^2}{16\pi^2}\int_0^1 dx\; (1-2x)^2 \ln\!\frac{m_T^2}{\mu^2}.
\]
The tensor prefactor \((p_\mu p_\nu - p^2\eta_{\mu\nu})\) vanishes at \(p=0\), hence
\[
\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0) = 0.
\]
Therefore no gauge-invariant Proca mass term \(m^2 A_\mu A^\mu\) is generated at one loop from a massive charged scalar.

\subsection{Massless charged fields}
If \(m_T=0\), regulate IR divergences (e.g., small mass \(m_T\to0^+\) or dimensional continuation). The structure remains transverse and any singular behavior is associated with long-range physics; no finite gauge-invariant mass term appears in the regulated limit.

\section{Mixed-sector diagrams and parametric suppression}
\subsection{Topology and power counting}
Mixed diagrams that could connect volumetric/longitudinal sectors to \(A_\mu\) require vertices that directly couple \(o\) or \(u_L\) to \(A_\mu\). In the minimal quadratic model such vertices are absent; if present they are higher-dimension and carry suppression factors (e.g., \(1/M^2\) or lattice-scale factors). Power counting shows mixed-loop contributions to \(\Pi_{\mu\nu}\) scale as \(e^2 g_{\text{mix}}^2 / M^{2n}\) with \(n\ge1\), hence parametrically small in the large-gap limit.

\subsection{Bounding mixed contributions}
For a representative mixed integrand with an explicit \(1/M^2\) factor, dimensional-regulator evaluation yields contributions bounded by
\[
|\delta\Pi_{\mu\nu}^{\text{mixed}}(p)| \le C\,\frac{e^2 g_{\text{mix}}^2}{M^2}\,(p^2 + \Lambda_{\text{IR}}^2)^{\alpha},
\]
with \(\alpha\ge0\) and constant \(C\) depending on loop integrals; thus in the IR \(p\to0\) these terms vanish as \(1/M^2\) (or faster) provided \(M\) is the largest scale.

\section{Ward identity and regulator choice}
\subsection{Ward identity}
Gauge invariance implies the Ward identity
\[
p^\mu \Pi_{\mu\nu}(p) = 0.
\]
Dimensional regularization preserves gauge invariance and ensures the algebraic manipulations above produce a transverse result. Regulators that break gauge invariance can introduce spurious longitudinal pieces which must be removed by gauge-restoring counterterms.

\section{Summary of the one-loop proof}
\subsection{Conclusions}
\begin{itemize}
  \item The one-loop vacuum polarization from a minimally coupled complex scalar is exactly transverse and can be written as \(\Pi_{\mu\nu}(p)=(p_\mu p_\nu - p^2\eta_{\mu\nu})\Pi_R(p^2)\) after renormalization.
  \item For massive charged excitations \(\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)=0\); no Proca mass is generated at one loop.
  \item Mixed-sector contributions that could induce a gauge mass are parametrically suppressed by powers of \(1/M^2\) when the volumetric gap \(M\) is large; explicit bounds follow from power counting and regulated loop integrals.
  \item Use of a gauge-preserving regulator (dimensional regularization) is essential to maintain the Ward identity and avoid spurious longitudinal artifacts.
\end{itemize}

%

\section{Objective}
Compute the one-loop vacuum polarization \(\Pi_{\mu\nu}(p)\) from a minimally coupled complex scalar field in dimensional regularization, demonstrate transversality \(p^\mu\Pi_{\mu\nu}(p)=0\), isolate the UV pole and perform \(\overline{\mathrm{MS}}\) renormalization, and evaluate the infrared limit \(\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)\) for \(m_T>0\).

\section{Model and Feynman rules}
\subsection{Euclidean action}
Consider the Euclidean action for a complex scalar \(\psi\) of charge \(e\) minimally coupled to \(A_\mu\):
\[
S[\psi,A]=\int d^D x\;\Big[|(\partial_\mu - i e A_\mu)\psi|^2 + m_T^2|\psi|^2\Big],
\]
with \(D=4-\epsilon\).

\subsection{Propagator and vertices}
\[
G_\psi(k)=\frac{1}{k^2 + m_T^2},
\qquad
V_\mu(k,k') = i e (k + k')_\mu,
\qquad
V_{\mu\nu}^{(2)} = 2 i e^2 \eta_{\mu\nu}.
\]

\section{One-loop diagrams}
\subsection{Bubble (vertex) diagram}
\[
\Pi_{\mu\nu}^{\text{bubble}}(p) = e^2 \int\!\frac{d^D k}{(2\pi)^D}\;
\frac{(2k+p)_\mu(2k+p)_\nu}{(k^2 + m_T^2)\big((k+p)^2 + m_T^2\big)}.
\]

\subsection{Seagull (contact) diagram}
\[
\Pi_{\mu\nu}^{\text{seagull}} = 2 e^2 \eta_{\mu\nu} \int\!\frac{d^D k}{(2\pi)^D}\;\frac{1}{k^2 + m_T^2}.
\]

\section{Feynman parameterization and momentum shift}
\subsection{Combine denominators}
Using a Feynman parameter \(x\in[0,1]\),
\[
\frac{1}{(k^2+m_T^2)\big((k+p)^2+m_T^2\big)}
= \int_0^1 dx\;\frac{1}{\big[(k+x p)^2 + \Delta\big]^2},
\]
with
\[
\Delta = m_T^2 + x(1-x)p^2.
\]

\subsection{Shift}
Set \(q=k+x p\). Then
\[
(2k+p)_\mu(2k+p)_\nu = 4 q_\mu q_\nu + 4\big(x-\tfrac12\big)p_{(\mu}q_{\nu)} + \big(1-4x(1-x)\big)p_\mu p_\nu.
\]
Terms linear in \(q\) vanish under symmetric integration.

\section{Tensor integrals in dimensional regularization}
\subsection{Standard integrals}
\[
\int\!\frac{d^D q}{(2\pi)^D}\;\frac{1}{(q^2+\Delta)^2}
= \frac{1}{(4\pi)^{D/2}}\frac{\Gamma(2-\tfrac{D}{2})}{\Gamma(2)}\Delta^{\tfrac{D}{2}-2},
\]
\[
\int\!\frac{d^D q}{(2\pi)^D}\;\frac{q_\mu q_\nu}{(q^2+\Delta)^2}
= \frac{\eta_{\mu\nu}}{D}\int\!\frac{d^D q}{(2\pi)^D}\;\frac{q^2}{(q^2+\Delta)^2},
\]
\[
\int\!\frac{d^D q}{(2\pi)^D}\;\frac{q^2}{(q^2+\Delta)^2}
= \frac{D}{2}\frac{1}{(4\pi)^{D/2}}\Gamma\!\Big(1-\tfrac{D}{2}\Big)\Delta^{\tfrac{D}{2}-1}.
\]

\subsection{Evaluate bubble}
Performing the \(q\)-integral and using the above identities,
\[
\Pi_{\mu\nu}^{\text{bubble}}(p)
= e^2 \int_0^1 dx\;\Bigg\{ \frac{4\eta_{\mu\nu}}{D} I_2(\Delta)
+ \big(1-4x(1-x)\big) p_\mu p_\nu\, I_0(\Delta)\Bigg\},
\]
where
\[
I_0(\Delta)=\int\!\frac{d^D q}{(2\pi)^D}\frac{1}{(q^2+\Delta)^2},\qquad
I_2(\Delta)=\int\!\frac{d^D q}{(2\pi)^D}\frac{q^2}{(q^2+\Delta)^2}.
\]

\section{Seagull evaluation and cancellation}
\subsection{Seagull integral}
\[
\Pi_{\mu\nu}^{\text{seagull}} = 2 e^2 \eta_{\mu\nu}\,J_0(m_T^2),
\qquad
J_0(m_T^2):=\int\!\frac{d^D k}{(2\pi)^D}\frac{1}{k^2+m_T^2}
= \frac{1}{(4\pi)^{D/2}}\Gamma\!\Big(1-\tfrac{D}{2}\Big)m_T^{D-2}.
\]

\subsection{Algebraic rearrangement}
Use the relation
\[
I_2(\Delta) = \frac{D}{2}\,I_0(\Delta)\,\Delta,
\]
and combine bubble and seagull contributions. After algebra,
\[
\Pi_{\mu\nu}(p) = \big(p_\mu p_\nu - p^2 \eta_{\mu\nu}\big)\,\Pi(p^2),
\]
with \(\Pi(p^2)\) given by a Feynman-parameter integral (explicit form below). The cancellation of \(\eta_{\mu\nu}\)-only terms is exact in dimensional regularization.

\section{Scalar form factor and pole isolation}
\subsection{Unrenormalized form factor}
One finds
\[
\Pi(p^2) = \frac{e^2}{(4\pi)^{D/2}}\int_0^1 dx\;\Gamma\!\Big(2-\tfrac{D}{2}\Big)
\left[\frac{(1-2x)^2}{2}\,\Delta^{\tfrac{D}{2}-2} - \frac{1}{D}\,\Delta^{\tfrac{D}{2}-1}\right],
\]
with \(\Delta=m_T^2+x(1-x)p^2\).

\subsection{Expand near \(D=4\)}
Set \(D=4-\epsilon\). Use
\[
\Gamma\!\Big(2-\tfrac{D}{2}\Big)=\Gamma\!\Big(\tfrac{\epsilon}{2}\Big)=\frac{2}{\epsilon}-\gamma_E + O(\epsilon),
\]
and
\[
\Delta^{\tfrac{D}{2}-2} = \Delta^{-\epsilon/2} = 1 - \tfrac{\epsilon}{2}\ln\Delta + O(\epsilon^2).
\]
Collect the pole term proportional to \(1/\epsilon\) and the finite part.

\section{Renormalization in \(\overline{\mathrm{MS}}\)}
\subsection{Pole subtraction}
The UV pole multiplies \((p_\mu p_\nu - p^2\eta_{\mu\nu})\) and is removed by a gauge-invariant wavefunction renormalization of \(A_\mu\). After \(\overline{\mathrm{MS}}\) subtraction the renormalized scalar form factor is
\[
\Pi_R(p^2) = \frac{e^2}{16\pi^2}\int_0^1 dx\; (1-2x)^2 \ln\!\frac{\Delta}{\mu^2},
\]
where \(\mu\) is the \(\overline{\mathrm{MS}}\) scale.

\subsection{Final renormalized vacuum polarization}
\[
\Pi_{\mu\nu}(p) = \big(p_\mu p_\nu - p^2 \eta_{\mu\nu}\big)\,\Pi_R(p^2).
\]

\section{Infrared limit}
\subsection{Evaluation at \(p=0\)}
At \(p=0\), \(\Delta=m_T^2\) and
\[
\Pi_R(0) = \frac{e^2}{16\pi^2}\ln\!\frac{m_T^2}{\mu^2}\int_0^1 dx\;(1-2x)^2.
\]
Since the tensor prefactor vanishes at \(p=0\),
\[
\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)=0.
\]

\subsection{Massless case}
If \(m_T=0\), regulate IR (e.g., small mass or analytic continuation). The structure remains transverse; regulated expressions show no finite gauge-invariant mass term is generated.

\section{Mixed-sector contributions and parametric suppression}
\subsection{Topology and power counting}
Mixed diagrams require vertices coupling \(o\) or \(u_L\) to \(A_\mu\). If present they are higher-dimension and carry suppression factors \(\sim 1/M^n\). Power counting yields at least one factor \(1/M^2\) for typical mixed topologies.

\subsection{Bound}
A representative bound for mixed contributions is
\[
\big|\delta\Pi_{\mu\nu}^{\text{mixed}}(p)\big| \le C\,\frac{e^2 g_{\text{mix}}^2}{M^2}\,(p^2 + \Lambda_{\text{IR}}^2)^\alpha,
\]
with \(\alpha\ge0\) and constant \(C\), showing suppression in the large-gap limit.

\section{Ward identity and regulator choice}
\subsection{Ward identity}
Gauge invariance implies
\[
p^\mu \Pi_{\mu\nu}(p) = 0.
\]
Dimensional regularization preserves this identity; regulators that break gauge invariance can introduce spurious longitudinal pieces which must be removed by gauge-restoring counterterms.

\section{Summary of results}
\subsection{Conclusions}
\begin{itemize}
  \item The one-loop vacuum polarization from a minimally coupled complex scalar is transverse and can be written as \(\Pi_{\mu\nu}(p)=(p_\mu p_\nu - p^2\eta_{\mu\nu})\Pi_R(p^2)\).
  \item After \(\overline{\mathrm{MS}}\) renormalization, \(\Pi_R(p^2)\) is finite and analytic near \(p^2=0\); hence \(\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)=0\) for \(m_T>0\).
  \item Mixed-sector contributions that could induce a gauge mass are parametrically suppressed by powers of \(1/M^2\) when the volumetric gap \(M\) is large.
  \item Use of a gauge-preserving regulator is essential to maintain the Ward identity and avoid spurious longitudinal artifacts.
\end{itemize}
